This is the exact inversion of the love-test. Lear demands that his vision of the proper order — love professed, kingdom divided on his terms — be ratified and followed; he is the messianic visionary Collins warns against, the charismatic sovereign whose imposed vision is "detrimental" in the most literal sense, detonating the kingdom. The visionary state he could have reached, had he learned his way through the loops to the humility of not-knowing, would have looked nothing like a vision and everything like a question: not "which of you shall we say doth love us most?" but "I do not understand enough to have the answers — what do you see that I cannot?" The visionary state is not the leader's vision imposed but the system's vision emerged; Lear demanded the first and detonated his kingdom, when the second was available only through the humility of the question he was constitutionally unable to ask.

1f.4  The Catalyst, Not the Clockmaker

The SAGE Handbook of Complexity and Management supplies the figure that gathers all of this into a single image, and it is an image this treatise has been circling under the name of "the programmer." Reviewing what complexity science does to our understanding of causality — reviving, in particular, Aristotle's marginalised formal and final causes against the modern fixation on efficient, billiard-ball causation — the Handbook's contributors (Alicia Juarrero foremost among them) conclude that the role of the leader of a complex system "is more like that of a catalyst than a clockmaker." The clockmaker stands outside the mechanism, designing it, winding it, controlling its every movement through direct efficient causation — the machine metaphor, the programmer, the Lear of the first scene who divides his realm as one might set the gears of a clock and expects it to keep the time he has set. The catalyst does something categorically different: it is within the system, and it works not by forcing outcomes but by altering the conditions under which the system organises itself, enabling reactions that then proceed by their own dynamics. The catalyst shapes the context — what Juarrero calls the "context-sensitive constraints" that are the modern form of Aristotle's formal cause — and then lets the system's own processes do the work, exactly as Cordelia's grain "limits the tree to an oak but does not specify it" (Part Ic's seed-as-formative-process).

Catalyst and clockmaker · the two theories of the king's job

The whole of King Lear, read through this distinction, is the collision of two incompatible theories of what a sovereign's job is. Lear holds the clockmaker theory: the king stands outside and above the kingdom, designing its order, winding its mechanism, controlling its parts through direct decree — and the love-test is the clockmaker's master-stroke, an attempt to set the entire future mechanism of the realm in a single afternoon's act of design. The play is the catastrophic refutation of the clockmaker theory and the brutal education of the king into the catalyst's world, where, in the SAGE contributors' words, "no one is ever in a position to control or design system operations" and "form emerges; it cannot be imposed." A catalyst-king would have understood his role as the shaping of conditions — the holding of a space, the setting of a few enabling constraints, the patient fostering of an order he could influence but never author — and would have known that the kingdom's future, like any complex system's, had to emerge from the interactions of all its parts and could not be stamped onto it from the throne. The reframing this treatise presses on every leader is exactly this: you are not the clockmaker of your organisation, however much the role tempts you to believe it; you are at most a catalyst within it, and the sooner you trade the fantasy of design for the discipline of enabling conditions, the sooner you stop, as Lear could not, mistaking the kingdom for a clock. The king is not the clockmaker of the realm but a catalyst within it; Lear staked everything on the clockmaker's fantasy of design, and the heath was the kingdom teaching him, too late, that form emerges and cannot be wound.

The clockmaker stands outside and winds the mechanism; the catalyst stands within and shapes the conditions — and the king who mistook the first for his job lost the kingdom to a form that could only ever emerge.
Complexity Coda · If

This part has drawn from Obolensky's Complex Adaptive Leadership and the SAGE Handbook of Complexity and Management the positive competence whose absence Part Ie diagnosed as the cult leader's tragedy. Polyarchy — leadership as a distributed dynamic, the both/and synthesis of oligarchy and anarchy — names the third state Lear never reaches, swinging instead between the throne's either and the storm's or, unable to perform "the paradox of leaders following." Letting go by holding back — Obolensky's 無為, "action through inaction," the held-back hand that lets the wobbling system find its own order — names the discipline Lear inverts, surrendering his place while clutching his control when true non-action surrenders the control and keeps the watchful place. Triple-loop learning names the path, through the questioning of the very frame, toward a visionary state that Collins's research and Obolensky's reading reveal to be not the charismatic leader's imposed vision — "absolutely not required… can be detrimental" — but the system's emergent orientation, reached only through the humility of the question Lear could not ask. And the catalyst, not the clockmaker names the figure that gathers them all: the leader within the system who shapes its context-sensitive constraints and lets form emerge, against the clockmaker-king who stands outside and winds the mechanism by decree. The weed-crown, once more, is the image at the centre: the clockmaker-king's design having failed, the kingdom reverts to the wild, self-organising, emergent growth that no throne ever authored — and the only sovereignty left to Lear on the heath is the catalyst's belated, broken understanding that form was always going to emerge from the field, and could never have been stamped upon it from above.

❀
Movement Two
The Sister Image and the Mythic Root
Beneath the crown, the older soil: Ophelia’s drowned garland as the gendered twin of Lear’s weeds, and the deposed corn-king whose sacrificial logic the flower scene inherits and breaks.

II. The Sister Scene: Ophelia and the Gendering of Botanical Madness

There is a willow grows aslant a brook,
That shows his hoar leaves in the glassy stream…
There with fantastic garlands did she come
Of crow-flowers, nettles, daisies, and long purples…
Her clothes spread wide,
And, mermaid-like, awhile they bore her up.— Hamlet, IV.vii — Gertrude reports Ophelia's flowered drowning

No discussion of Lear's flowers can proceed honestly without its necessary companion: Ophelia's. The two scenes are separated by perhaps five or six years of Shakespeare's writing life — Hamlet generally dated to 1600–1601, Lear to 1605–1606 — and by a difference in gender that determines almost everything about how each image was subsequently received, painted, operatically set, and clinically photographed. Where Lear's flowers are a crown, an act of self-coronation by a deposed sovereign, Ophelia's flowers are a distribution, an act of gift-giving by a woman whose madness articulates itself through care for others even as her own self dissolves. The difference between crowning oneself and giving away one's garland is, this section argues, the difference between two entire traditions of representing unreason — one essentially political, the other essentially elegiac — and Western culture's botanical madness-imagery has been organized along this gendered fault line ever since.

2.1  The Distribution Scene

Ophelia's flower scene (Hamlet, 4.5) gives each bloom a named addressee and a stated meaning, in a way Lear's never does. There's rosemary; that's for remembrance. Pray you, love, remember. And there is pansies; that's for thoughts, she says, continuing through fennel, columbines, rue, and a daisy, before noting that the violets withered all when my father died. Laertes' verdict — A document in madness — thoughts and remembrance fitted — gives this treatise's opening section its working epigraph, for it names precisely the paradox Edgar names in Lear's scene with his "reason in madness": that the mad utterance is not noise but a text, a "document," requiring only the right reader.

The crucial structural difference is directional. Lear's flowers move toward the self: he gathers and crowns himself, an act of auto-coronation that restages, grotesquely, the very ceremony of investiture the play's opening scene had already perverted by turning love into a competitive performance for territorial reward. Ophelia's flowers move outward, toward others — Laertes, the absent Queen, herself last of all ("here's some for me") — in a gesture that, however broken, remains social, communicative, oriented toward relationship even as the speaker's own coherence collapses. Object Studies' commentary on the scene observes that there is no folly in what Ophelia is suggesting by presenting these flowers, that the flowers are embedded in some deeper context that allows Ophelia to look at them as symbols of larger ideas. The same cannot quite be said of Lear's gathering, which is indiscriminate by design — fumitory beside hemlock, cuckooflower beside darnel — precisely because Lear's madness is organized around the loss of discrimination as such, the inability any longer to tell daughter from daughter, loyalty from flattery, kingdom from self.

The seed in the dissolving · Shaw's pattern that forms as another breaks

Ophelia distributes flowers — seeds, in the literal botanical sense — at the very moment her own coherence is dissolving, and Patricia Shaw supplies the exact image for why this is not merely poignant but structurally precise. Watching a group improvise a word-association game until each pattern collapsed, Shaw noticed that "there was always a seed of a new pattern forming as the previous one dissolved," and that this seed was "bafflingly difficult to locate — it was not 'in' either word, nor between two words," but emerged across the dissolution itself, recognisable only in retrospect. Ophelia's giving-away is precisely this: the seed of a new pattern (remembrance, thought, grace, distributed outward to others) forming in the very act of her own dissolution, located nowhere you can point to — not in the rosemary, not in the rue, but in the dispersing gesture itself. This is the deepest answer to why her madness reads as gift rather than mere collapse: she is, in Shaw's terms, the seed forming as the pattern breaks, the system scattering exactly what might re-root elsewhere. Lear hoards his weeds into a crown; Ophelia sows hers. One enacts dissolution as accumulation, the other as dispersal — and only dispersal leaves seeds.

As one pattern dissolves a seed of the next is already forming — located in no single word, but in the dispersal itself.

2.2  Millais, and the Invention of a Visual Genre

If Lear's flower scene has resisted painterly capture — there is no single canonical Lear-in-flowers painting that occupies cultural memory the way one Ophelia painting does — Ophelia's drowning produced, in John Everett Millais's 1851–52 canvas, perhaps the single most reproduced image of female madness in the Western tradition. The Tate's account of the work's genesis records Millais's own excitement at sourcing material: he wrote of purchasing a really splendid lady's ancient dress, all flowered over in silver embroidery, costing old and dirty as it is, four pounds, a material detail that already tells us something about the Pre-Raphaelite method: madness rendered through the most scrupulous possible fidelity to surface, to the textile and botanical real.

The painting's flowers are not arbitrary. Wikipedia's summary of the iconography notes that the flowers shown floating on the river were chosen to correspond with Shakespeare's description of Ophelia's garland, and that they also reflect the Victorian interest in the "language of flowers," according to which each flower carries a symbolic meaning. The poppy, conspicuously not mentioned by Shakespeare's description of the scene, is Millais's addition, and it represents sleep and death — the painter supplementing the playwright's own botanical catalogue with a flower whose Victorian semiotic charge (opium, oblivion, the long Romantic association of poppies with narcotic forgetting) the original text does not supply but which nineteenth-century viewers would have read instantly.

What Millais accomplishes, and what generations of subsequent Ophelia-painters after him would repeat with variation, is the conversion of a stage direction's implied violence (madness, drowning, the body given over to the river's botany) into an image of extraordinary visual calm. Artnet's account of the painting's reception records that contemporary viewers, and later critics, found in the canvas a vivid, psychologically charged image in which Shakespeare's offstage death — for Ophelia's drowning, unlike Lear's flower-crowning, happens nowhere onstage in the original text, but is only reported by Gertrude — becomes paradoxically more visible, more available to sustained looking, than it ever could have been in the theatre. This is the first of several inversions this treatise will track: an image that the original dramatist deliberately kept offstage, narrated rather than shown, becomes in its visual afterlife the single most-seen image associated with the entire play.

Mia White Media's account of the painting's critical reception traces the figure's afterlife with precision: today, any image of a young woman in the water, either with flowers or a long flowy dress can be considered Ophelian, and the critic Elaine Showalter is cited concluding that there is no "true" Ophelia for whom feminist criticism must unambiguously speak, but perhaps only a Cubist Ophelia of multiple perspectives, more than the sum of all her parts. The flower-madness image, in other words, detached itself from its textual origin almost immediately upon visual codification and became a free-floating cultural template — applicable to any drowned, flowered, dishevelled woman regardless of textual warrant. No equivalent detachment occurred for Lear; his flower-crowned figure remained, and largely remains, tethered to its specific dramatic source. This asymmetry is itself a finding, and this treatise will return to it: female botanical madness became iconographically portable in a way male botanical madness did not, a fact with consequences for everything from Victorian asylum photography to contemporary fashion's continued recourse to the drowned-in-flowers woman as a stock image, with no comparably stable counterpart drawn from Lear.

2.3  Dalí's Doubled Inheritance

The line from Millais to twentieth-century surrealism is not metaphorical but direct and documented. Artnet's profile of the painting notes that the image has influenced modern artists like the Surrealist Salvador Dalí, who also produced an iteration of the scene titled "Ophelia's Death" (1973). This is a significant hinge point for the present treatise's larger argument, because it means the very artist whose floral suites this treatise will examine in Part IV as the major twentieth-century vehicle for vegetal-human metamorphosis had already, more than a decade prior, engaged directly with the specific iconographic tradition — Ophelia, flowers, water, dissolution of self into botanical surround — that sits as Lear's gendered sister-image. Dalí's career thus contains, within itself, both halves of the split tradition this section describes: the Millais-derived, essentially elegiac, feminized flower-madness of "Ophelia's Death," and the separate, later, more aggressively metamorphic floral suites (FlorDalí, 1968–69; Les Fruits, 1969; Florals, 1972) that this treatise's Part IV will argue belong to a different, more properly Lear-adjacent register — botanical invasion of form as such, rather than botanical elegy for a particular drowned woman.

2.4  Why the Asymmetry Matters

The governing question this section leaves for the rest of the treatise is this: why has Western visual and performance culture produced an entire durable genre — "the Ophelia" — out of female botanical madness, while producing no equivalent durable genre out of Lear's male botanical madness, despite the textual image being, if anything, more structurally central to its play (Ophelia's drowning is reported, not shown; Lear's flower-crowning is staged, fully visible, line after line of major dramatic business) and more politically freighted (Lear's scene contains the play's most explicit social criticism; Ophelia's contains grief and implication rather than argument)?

Part of the answer is straightforwardly art-historical: the nineteenth century's appetite for paintable, beautifiable female death found no comparable appetite for paintable, beautifiable male senility. Part of the answer is theoretical, in the sense Michel Foucault's later analysis of madness's gendering will supply in Part IX of this treatise: madness coded as passive, beautiful, succumbing — as Ophelia's is — could be aestheticized by a patriarchal visual culture in a way that madness coded as active, raving, and politically articulate — as Lear's is, full of imperatives, accusations, "kill, kill, kill, kill, kill, kill!" — resisted. Ophelia could be looked at. Lear, dressed fantastically and saying true things about justice and power, demanded instead to be listened to, and the history of art has always found listening harder to paint than looking.

Edge of chaos · the silenced channel

There is a complexity reading of why the culture could aestheticise Ophelia and not Lear, and it is sharper than the art-historical one. Ophelia, drowning, is a system collapsing quietly toward Kauffman's steam — dissolution without resistance, the disordered zone entered without protest, paintable precisely because it makes no counter-claim. Lear raving is a system at the edge of chaos, generating novel and unwelcome order — the “reason in madness” that indicts the very power looking at him. A patriarchal visual culture could safely frame the first because dissolution threatens no one; it flinched from the second because edge-of-chaos lucidity is the most dangerous output a system produces, the truth the legitimate order was built not to hear. The shadow system speaks in two registers — the silence that withdraws and the candour that accuses — and a culture will always find the withdrawing silence easier to hang on a gallery wall than the accusing voice.

Dissolution can be made beautiful; it is emergence that frightens the court.
Complexity Coda · II

Ophelia's flowers move outward, named and addressed to others; Lear's move inward, gathered for himself alone. Change-management practice has its own version of this directional split, and it tracks reliably onto who gets heard during a transformation and who is merely watched through it. Programmes built around a single sponsoring executive's announced vision distribute meaning the way Lear distributes his crown — self-referentially, without negotiation, indifferent to whether the surrounding system can actually metabolise what is being imposed on it. The practice Kevin Flinn's A Web of Relations develops for facilitating organisational change leans deliberately toward Ophelia's directionality instead: meaning made and exchanged in conversation between people, distributed rather than self-crowned, so that whatever new pattern emerges belongs to the relationships that produced it rather than to the single figure who announced it. An organisation, like a play's audience, will reliably find it easier to watch a leader's vision than to listen for the quieter, more distributed sense-making already under way among the people who will have to live inside whatever changes.

Mead's account of the gesture-response social act sharpens this part's directional contrast further. A gesture, for Mead, only becomes meaningful in the response it provokes — meaning is made between parties, in the loop of call and answer, never delivered whole by one party to a passive other. Ophelia's flower-giving is gesture in Mead's full sense: each bloom offered into a relationship, its meaning awaiting Laertes's, the Queen's, the audience's response to complete it. Lear's self-coronation has no such interlocutor; it is gesture addressed to no one, a call issued into a void that cannot answer, which is precisely why Edgar's cry — "O thou side-piercing sight!" — matters so much structurally: it is the response Lear's own gesture could not generate for itself, supplied from outside by the one witness the scene happens to provide. A change communication addressed downward without expectation of an answering gesture is, on this Meadian reading, not communication at all but a form of Lear's own self-coronation: meaning the sender alone has decided, awaiting no genuine response to become complete.

III. The Deposed Corn-King: Frazer, Sacrificial Kingship, and the Logic of Vegetal Sovereignty

The old king of the wood had to be slain by his successor… for the divine life, incarnate in a material and mortal body, was liable to be tainted and corrupted by the weakness of the frail medium in which it was for a time enshrined.— James Frazer, The Golden Bough (1890)

Before this treatise can responsibly trace the flower-crowned Lear forward into modernity, it must trace him backward into the deep anthropological substrate that James George Frazer's The Golden Bough (1890–1915) systematized, however contestably, at the threshold of the twentieth century. Frazer's framework is not offered here as literal source material for Shakespeare — there is no evidence the playwright knew any such comparative mythology, since Frazer's synthesis postdates him by nearly three centuries — but as a way of making visible a structural logic latent in the play's own imagery, a logic that later readers, directors, and artists, having absorbed Frazer (consciously or by cultural osmosis) into the available interpretive vocabulary of the twentieth century, would explicitly apply back onto Lear.

3.1  The King of the Wood

Frazer's argument, distilled to its structural essentials, holds that a great range of ancient and folk-religious practices across Europe and beyond can be understood through the figure of a "sacred king" or "King of the Wood," a human being who represented a solar deity in a periodically re-enacted fertility rite, who represented the spirit of vegetation, a divine John Barleycorn, and whose life cycle mirrored the agricultural year exactly: he came into being in the spring, reigned during the summer, and ritually died at harvest time, only to be reborn at the winter solstice. Crucially, this king's authority was never independent of the vegetation he stood for; Frazer's encyclopedia gloss notes that the sacred king's powers were bound by sympathetic restriction — there is a sympathetic connection between the king and his kingdom. The ruler is subject to restrictions to help preserve it — meaning the king's body and the land's fertility were, in this anthropological imagination, a single continuous substance, such that what happened to one happened, necessarily, to the other.

The Grokipedia summary of the related Holly King/Oak King folklore complex names the specific ritual form most relevant to the Lear image: May Day kings, where a youth was crowned as the embodiment of vegetation and subjected to mock executions to mimic the spirit's decline at harvest. The crowning-in-vegetation and the ritual deposition are, in this folkloric grammar, two faces of a single ceremony, not two separate events. To crown a king in flowers and weeds is already, in this much older symbolic economy, to mark him for sacrifice — the floral crown is not an honour bestowed upon continuing sovereignty but a sign that sovereignty's seasonal term has reached its harvest-point and must now be cut down so that new growth can follow.

3.2  Lear as Failed Corn-King

Read against this framework, the flower-crowned Lear of Act 4 becomes legible as a corn-king ritual gone catastrophically wrong — performed, that is, by an old man who does not understand that he is performing it, who believes himself still continuously sovereign ("I am the king himself") at precisely the moment the play's own symbolic grammar is marking him, through vegetation, for the sacrificial harvest his daughters have already, in the world of plot, set in motion. The corn-king mythos requires a willing or at least comprehending sacrifice, timed to the agricultural year, productive of renewal. Lear's flower-crowning offers none of these conditions: it is involuntary, untimed (the play gives us storm and field but no calendar), and productive of nothing but further catastrophe — Cordelia's death, his own, the kingdom's devastation rather than its renewal. Shakespeare, this section argues, has taken the available folk-ritual grammar of vegetal kingship — a grammar his audience would have known, however dimly, through residual May Day customs, harvest-lore, and the broader European folk-memory Frazer would later systematize — and staged its failure. The play gives us the corn-king image with the corn-king's redemptive logic deliberately withheld.

This withholding is, this treatise contends, precisely what licenses the image's subsequent and very wide cultural propagation. A ritual that works, that completes its cycle and delivers renewal, tends toward closure — it is performed, the harvest comes, the story ends. A ritual that fails, that is staged with full ceremonial weight (crown, vegetation, public madness, an audience of horrified witnesses in Edgar and Gloucester) but produces no harvest, no renewal, nothing but further ruin, remains permanently open, available for re-staging precisely because it was never discharged the first time. Every subsequent artist who returns to the flower-crowned Lear is, on this reading, attempting to discharge a ritual debt the play itself incurred and refused to pay.

3.3  Darnel as Anti-Sacrament

One further detail from the Frazerian corpus sharpens the point. Frazer's catalogue of vegetation-deity practices across cultures, summarized by the Encyclopedia.com entry, repeatedly emphasizes corn and grain specifically as the privileged vegetal medium of sacred kingship — Demeter, Osiris, the corn-mother figure shared across multiple mythological systems, the literal bread of the harvest standing in continuous metonymic relation to the body of the god or king who guarantees it. C. S. Lewis, in commentary recorded by one biographer working through Frazer's influence on Lewis's own theology, observed that the corn-king mythos lies disturbingly close to the Eucharist itself: you had a dying God, who was always representative of the corn: you see Him holding the corn, that is, bread, in His hand, and saying, "This is My Body".

Against this sacramental background, the specific botanical inventory Cordelia gives for her father's crown reads as a kind of anti-sacrament. Darnel, as established in Part I, is not the corn itself but a parasite that infests and degrades corn — a common grass that sometimes grows as a weed in corn crops. Lear's crown is therefore composed not of the sacred grain that should mark him as a true corn-king bearing the kingdom's fertility forward, but of the grain's own enemies and waste-products: the weeds that choke sustaining corn, that represent agricultural failure rather than agricultural plenty. Where the mythic corn-king is crowned in the very substance of harvest and bears that substance forward into renewal, Lear is crowned in harvest's opposite — infestation, waste, "idle weeds" — and this is, this treatise argues, the precise mechanism by which Shakespeare converts a potentially consoling mythic structure into one of the most devastating images of failed sovereignty in the dramatic literature of any language.

Pioneer species · the weed-crown as the vanguard of succession

And yet there is a science of weeds that turns even this reading inside out, and it has been hiding in the botany the whole time. Every single plant in Lear's crown — fumitory, the furrow-weeds, burdock, nettles, cuckoo-flower, darnel — belongs to the ecological category botanists call ruderals or pioneer species: the plants that colonise disturbed, broken, bare, or damaged ground first, before any stable community can establish itself. These are precisely the species that appear on the cleared building site, the burnt field, the abandoned furrow, the churned earth — the opportunists that rush into disturbance, fix the broken ground, and begin the long self-organising process that ecologists call succession: the unforced, emergent sequence by which bare earth becomes, over time and without any designer, first weeds, then grasses, then scrub, then woodland, then mature forest. McMillan's complexity science names the engine of this exactly: living systems are "self-renewing" because they "dissipate their energy in order to renew or recreate themselves," and disturbance is what "disturbs the system leading to the creation of new life forms." Read against succession ecology, Lear's weed-crown is no longer simply the emblem of waste and agricultural failure; it is the vanguard of renewal — the first colonisers rushing into the disturbed ground of his shattered sovereignty, the pioneer species that always appear first when an established order has been cleared. The same plants that read, sacramentally, as the anti-harvest read, ecologically, as the necessary first stage of every regeneration: nothing grows on bare ground except weeds, and nothing ever became a forest without the weeds coming first. This is the deepest doubleness of the crown. The weeds are what grows when the cultivated order has failed — and they are also, inescapably, the only thing from which a new order can begin, because succession has no other starting material. The weeds that crown the ruin of the king are the pioneer species of whatever grows next; on the disturbed ground of a fallen order, the first colonisers always look like failure and are always, in fact, the beginning of the forest.

Weeds are the pioneer species of disturbed ground — the first to colonise the ruin, the necessary vanguard of every succession; the crown of failure is also the seedbed of the forest that has not yet come.
The art of our necessities · vile things made precious

And yet the play supplies, in Lear's own mouth, the principle by which harvest's waste becomes the most precious image in the canon. On the heath he tells his Fool: The art of our necessities is strange, that can make vile things precious. The weed-crown is the proof of its own maxim — the field's vilest refuse, darnel and hemlock and parasite, transmuted by the art of Lear's necessity into the one crown four centuries cannot stop painting. This is the corn-king mythos inverted twice over: not the sacred grain bearing fertility forward, but the grain's waste; and yet not waste left worthless, but waste made, by necessity's strange art, into something priceless. Complexity science calls this emergence — the edge of chaos making genuine novelty from constraint and pressure — but Shakespeare said it first and shorter, and gave it to a madman in a storm.

The corn-king's harvest renews the land; Lear's harvest of weeds renews the image — for the art of necessity makes the vile precious.

3.4  The Twentieth Century Rediscovers Its Own Folklore

It is no accident that the same mid-twentieth-century moment that produced Jan Kott's absurdist rereading of Lear (treated at length in Part VIII below) also produced a renewed scholarly and popular interest in Frazerian vegetation-myth, mediated through T. S. Eliot's explicit Frazer-and-Weston debts in The Waste Land and through the broad mid-century vogue for myth-and-ritual criticism associated with Northrop Frye, Francis Fergusson, and the Cambridge ritualists. Directors staging Lear in this intellectual climate — Peter Brook foremost among them, whose 1962 Stratford production and 1971 film both drew on a stripped, almost ritual bareness of design — were working in a cultural moment primed to see the flower-crowned king not merely as a pathetic individual case of senile dementia but as the visible failure of an entire mythic apparatus of seasonal renewal. The bleakness for which Brook's Lear became notorious — Pauline Kael's famous denunciation, I didn't just dislike this production, I hated it!, suggesting the alternative title Night of the Living Dead — is, on this reading, not simply a directorial mood choice but the logical endpoint of taking the corn-king substrate seriously and refusing it any of the consolations the original folk-ritual would have supplied. If the ritual fails and stays failed, Kael's nightmare title is not hyperbole; it is structurally accurate. A corn-king who does not die and rise, who simply dies, leaves behind exactly the kind of wasteland Brook's bare white stage and frozen Jutland landscapes were built to stage.

3.5  Why the Ritual Calendar Is the Thing That Fails

It is worth pausing, before this treatise moves on, over exactly what kind of failure Frazer's corn-king mythos undergoes once Shakespeare's plot has finished with it, because the failure is structurally specific rather than merely tonal. What fails is not vegetal sovereignty as such — the play gives Lear his crown of weeds regardless, the imagery arrives on schedule, the king is in every visual respect "fantastically dressed" precisely as the genre requires — but the calendar the ritual depends on to make its sacrifice meaningful: the assumed synchrony between an old order's scheduled death and a new order's guaranteed succession, the assumed agreement among all parties, including the sacrificed king himself, about what the ceremony means and what it will produce. Frazer's actual May Day kings are crowned by a community that has already, collectively and explicitly, agreed on the ceremony's terms; the mock execution that follows is mock precisely because everyone watching knows in advance that spring will return regardless of the particular youth currently wearing the crown. Lear's catastrophe is that no such collective agreement underwrites his own crowning. Goneril and Regan have not agreed to a ritual; they have pursued, each independently and in escalating competition with the other, an actual seizure of actual power, and the play gives no indication that either daughter understands herself to be enacting Frazer's fertility rite rather than simply taking what she believes the love-test entitles her to. The ceremony's form survives Shakespeare's plot intact — crown, vegetation, public display — but the calendar's underlying social agreement, the thing that made Frazer's actual May Day ritual safe to stage because everyone present knew spring was coming regardless, has been removed, leaving the form to operate with no guarantee at all about what, if anything, will follow.

The sandpile · the single grain that brings down the field

There is a deeper reason the grain-field is the right setting for this catastrophe, and it comes from the branch of complexity science Per Bak called self-organised criticality — the study, conducted first on literal piles of sand and grain, of how systems organise themselves to a critical state in which a single added grain can trigger an avalanche of any size. Drop grains one at a time onto a pile and it steepens until it reaches the angle of repose, the critical slope at which it hovers permanently on the edge of collapse; thereafter each new grain may do nothing, or may set off a slide of two grains, or twenty, or the whole face of the pile, and — this is the law that makes the model profound — there is no way, even in principle, to predict from the grain which size of avalanche will follow. Stacey and Mowles describe exactly this signature in the systems they study: at the edge of chaos a system produces "avalanches of destruction… with many small and few large extinction events," the unmistakable power-law fingerprint of a pile poised at criticality. Lear's kingdom is such a pile, and the love-test has steepened it to the critical angle: the ageing king, the competitive daughters, the divided map, the assembled court — a structure loaded grain by grain to the edge of repose, hovering on collapse before the first word is spoken. And Cordelia's "Nothing" is the single grain. Not a large cause — one quiet word, the smallest possible refusal — but dropped onto a pile already at criticality, and the avalanche it triggers takes the entire face of the kingdom down: banishment, storm, war, the deaths of all three daughters and the king. The grain that brings down the field is not larger than the grains that did nothing; the field was simply ready, and the avalanche was always going to be triggered by whatever grain fell next — which is why Cordelia's smallest honesty and the kingdom's total ruin are the same event.

A kingdom steepened to the critical angle waits only for the next grain; Cordelia's "Nothing" was not a great cause but the smallest one, and the pile was already poised to fall.

This is, this treatise suggests, the single most useful thing Frazer's anthropology can be made to teach a reader interested in organisational change rather than in folk religion: that the reassuring calendar most planned transformation efforts assume — the announced end-date, the named successor structure, the implicit guarantee that the old order's deposition will be followed, on schedule, by the new order's installation — is exactly the kind of collective, pre-agreed ritual consensus Frazer's actual May Day kings enjoyed and Lear's own court, fractured by the love-test's competitive logic before the storm even arrives, conspicuously does not. A change programme can borrow every external form of Frazer's ritual — the announcement, the ceremony, the visible marking of old authority's end — while lacking entirely the collective agreement about meaning and succession that made the original ritual survivable for everyone involved, including the figure being ritually deposed. The result, when this happens, is not Frazer's safely cyclical sacrifice but Lear's: a deposition that looks ceremonially complete and is, underneath the ceremony, completely open, with no guaranteed harvest waiting on the other side of it.

Fire suppression · the cycle denied returns as conflagration

Elizabeth McMillan supplies a case that turns the corn-king's denied cycle into a precise complexity-science warning. For over a century, she records, the US Park Service "maintained equilibrium" in Yellowstone "by quickly extinguishing fires, denying the natural rhythm of fire and re-growth whereby forests cleanse and renew themselves" — until, in 1988, the suppressed deadfall and debris that had "built up on the forest floor" fed a single set of lightning strikes into a conflagration that destroyed a quarter of the park, burning so hot it "charred the living components of topsoil that would otherwise have survived." The cycle denied does not disappear; it accumulates as fuel, and returns catastrophically. McMillan draws on Margaret Wheatley to name the root error precisely: "we have confused control with order." This is the corn-king's lesson and Lear's catastrophe in a single image. Lear, like the Park Service, confuses control with order: he tries to control the succession — suppressing the natural cycle of ageing and inheritance through the engineered love-test, extinguishing the small honest fire of Cordelia's "Nothing" and Kent's candour before it can spread — and the suppressed cycle returns, exactly as at Yellowstone, as the all-consuming conflagration of the storm and the war, burning down to the charred topsoil of the final act. The weeds that crown him are the deadfall of a hundred suppressed seasons. The honest small fire extinguished becomes the conflagration that takes everything; control is not order but the deferral and concentration of the cycle you refused to let burn.

Confuse control with order and you do not prevent the fire — you store it; the cycle denied its small seasonal burning returns as the conflagration that chars the very soil.
Complexity Coda · III

Frazer's corn-king is deposed on a fixed ritual calendar, his death scheduled and sanctified in advance so that the community can be certain a successor will follow; Lear's deposition has no such calendar and no such certainty, which is exactly why this part read the play as Frazer's ritual structure left deliberately unredeemed. Most change-management literature still assumes something closer to Frazer's confidence than to Lear's chaos: a sponsor announces the end of the old order, a successor structure is named in the same breath, and the interval between old king and new is treated as a brief, manageable, calendrically bounded rite of passage. Stacey and Mowles's complex responsive processes literature insists this confidence is almost always unearned. An organisation's "old order" rarely dies on schedule and its replacement rarely arrives fully formed; what actually happens in the interval is closer to Lear's unscripted wandering than to the corn-king's choreographed sacrifice — an uncertain, locally negotiated, frequently undignified period in which the system's new pattern has to be discovered through the interactions of the people living through it, not simply installed by the figure who announced its necessity.

Elias's concept of the figuration supplies a sharper vocabulary than Frazer's anthropology alone can offer for what actually distinguishes the corn-king's death from Lear's. A figuration, for Elias, is constituted by a fluctuating balance of power among interdependent people — not a possession any single party holds, but a structural property of the relationship itself. Frazer's corn-king dies inside a figuration whose power-ratios are fixed in advance by ritual consensus: everyone, including the king, agrees in advance on the terms of the succession the death enacts. Lear's deposition happens inside a figuration whose power-ratios have already shifted unpredictably and violently — Goneril and Regan's combined power exceeding what either anticipated when the love-test began, Lear's own power collapsing faster than his self-conception can track it — and it is this untracked, emergent shift in the figuration's actual balance, rather than any ritual consensus, that produces his fall. Change programmes that model an organisation's power relations as fixed quantities to be reallocated by announcement, rather than as a figuration whose ratios are already shifting in ways no announcement controls, are modelling Frazer's corn-king when they should be modelling Lear.

❦
Movement Three
The Image Across the Arts
A single stage direction refracted through surrealism, cinema, opera, butoh, the absurd, the clinical photograph, the comic panel, and the couture runway — each medium discovering the same vegetal undoing of the sovereign body.

IV. The Surrealist Garden: Dalí, Ernst, Tanning, and the Vegetal Body

As wild geese that the creeping fowler eye,
Or russet-pated choughs, many in sort,
Rising and cawing at the gun’s report…— A Midsummer Night’s Dream, III.ii — the surreal logic of transformation

If Part II established Ophelia as the feminized, elegiac half of botanical madness's cultural inheritance, this part argues that twentieth-century surrealism furnishes the genuinely Lear-adjacent half: not flowers given in elegy, but flowers as the vehicle of bodily metamorphosis itself, the human form actively dissolving into and being reconstituted by vegetal material. Surrealism's relationship to flowers is, on the evidence assembled below, structurally identical to Lear's relationship to his field-gathered crown — an embrace of vegetal disorder as the price, and the revelation, of escaping the disciplined, hierarchical, "reasonable" body.

4.1  Dalí's Theory of Vegetal Metamorphosis

Salvador Dalí stated his governing principle with unusual directness, in language the Dalí Museum's curatorial materials preserve: I see the human form in trees, leaves, animals. I see animal and vegetal characteristics in humans, he said, continuing: human beings create and change. When they sleep, they change totally — into flowers, plants, trees. This is not a casual aesthetic preference but a fully worked theory of metamorphic identity, one that treats the boundary between human form and vegetal form as porous, temporary, and — crucially for the Lear comparison — most permeable precisely at the threshold of unconsciousness, of sleep's surrender of waking discrimination. Dalí's late-career floral suites — FlorDalí (1968–69), FlorDalí: Les Fruits (1969), and Surrealist Flowers (1972) — translate this theory into print series of considerable strangeness. The Dalí Museum's director described the project in terms that could, with minimal adjustment, describe Lear's own field-gathered self-coronation: Dalí's botanical series are an exercise in this same intervention, a Surrealist collage to make a new phylum of beings, a new species of perception.

Art & Object's account of the Denver Botanic Gardens exhibition of these suites notes their tonal range runs from the whimsy of FlorDalí to the more mysterious, even violent FlorDalí - Les Fruits, and quotes curator Peter Tush's description of the works as humorously composite plant creatures that grow in landscapes with topographies reminiscent of the craggy Spanish coast. The crucial word here is composite: these are not paintings of flowers, nor paintings of human figures merely surrounded by flowers, but images in which floral and figural material has been spliced into single, irreducible hybrid forms — precisely the operation Lear's own crown performs upon his body, transforming a deposed king into a composite figure who is neither cleanly human-sovereign nor cleanly vegetal-debris but some unstable, "fantastically dressed" third thing.

4.2  The Guide to Flower Themes: A Taxonomy

A useful taxonomic survey of surrealist floral motifs identifies several distinct registers that map with surprising precision onto distinct moments within Lear's own flower-scene and its surrounding dramaturgy. Fini's women with floral hair, symbolizing metamorphosis and mysticism supplies one register — flowers as crown, as the literal coronal position on the head, which is of course exactly Lear's own staging (he is "crowned," not merely accompanied by, his weeds). A second register, exemplified by Dalí's melting, almost fleshy flower forms [that] evoke both attraction and repulsion, supplies the affective ambivalence Edgar's "side-piercing sight" already names: a simultaneous pull toward and recoil from the spectacle. A third register is named with particular precision by the same survey: instead of being symbols of life, surrealist flowers often appear wilted, fossilized, or menacing, exemplified by Tanning's sunflowers that seem to have a disturbing presence, bordering on the supernatural. This third register is the one Lear's own crown most exactly inhabits — not flowers in their conventional symbolic register of life, fertility, and beauty, but flowers as menace, as the visible sign of a sustaining order (Cordelia's "sustaining corn") gone toxic and parasitic.

The same survey extends its inventory directly into surrealist cinema, noting that in Un Chien Andalou (1929), directed with Dalí, flowers appear in surreal, disjointed sequences, reinforcing the film's disruptive dream logic, while in The Exterminating Angel (1962), dying flowers symbolize societal decay and entrapment — a film whose plot, a group of dinner guests inexplicably unable to leave a room until social order itself disintegrates, bears a structural family resemblance to Lear's own plot of social hierarchy collapsing once its presiding authority can no longer maintain the fiction of order. Magritte's contribution to the same taxonomy is described as paradox rather than menace: The Tomb of the Wrestlers (1961) depicts a giant flower filling an empty room, subverting scale and reality — an image of botanical excess overwhelming domestic and bodily proportion that anticipates, almost programmatically, the scale-violation latent in Lear's own image, an old man's entire visible identity overwritten by the field's vegetal surplus.

4.3  Ernst's Herbarium and the Botanist's Method Turned Strange

Max Ernst's engagement with plant material took a more procedurally rigorous, almost scientific form than Dalí's, and is for that reason worth treating separately. The blog Herbarium World, written from inside the discipline of botanical illustration rather than art history proper, observes that Ernst employed a technique that is occasionally seen on specimen sheets or in botanists' notebooks: rubbings of leaves or other structures, producing in his 1925 series Histoire Naturelle images built from a wood rubbing to create a leaf, or a leaf rubbing a tree — a deliberate confusion of botanical categories executed with the same procedural fidelity a real herbarium specimen sheet would demand. The effect is structurally close to what this treatise has already identified, in Part I, as Cordelia's catalogue: a botanically precise inventory deployed not to stabilize identification (as a real herbarium does) but to destabilize it, to produce, through the appearance of scientific rigor, an experience of taxonomic vertigo.

The LibreTexts survey of Dada and Surrealism notes a further, darker development in Ernst's vegetal-erotic imagery, observing that Max Ernst's ironically titled The Joy of Life shows [praying mantis] insects in the foreground of an ominous jungle scene filled with half-hidden, menacing creatures, part of a broader surrealist fascination with the praying mantis as a figure for the era's anxieties about feminine sexuality and violence. This insect-vegetal continuum — jungle, mantis, half-hidden menace — extends the floral-menace register identified above into the realm of the entomological, suggesting that surrealism's engagement with "nature" in general, not flowers narrowly, repeatedly returns to the same structural device this treatise has been tracking in Lear: nature as the site where human categories of safety, hierarchy, and discrimination dissolve.

4.4  The Limits of the Comparison

Honesty about the comparison's limits requires acknowledging where the surrealist vegetal-body tradition departs from Lear's own. Surrealism's broader catalogue of bodily metamorphosis ranges across registers this treatise does not wish to collapse into a single undifferentiated "vegetal-surreal" category — Tanning's uncanny, almost sentient sunflowers and Dalí's melting floral forms belong to a register of estranged but self-directed transformation considerably closer to Lear's own staging than other strands of the same movement, which pursue transformation as something inflicted upon, rather than undergone by, the body depicted. Lear's scene, crucially, stages an old man's self-directed bodily transformation — he gathers and crowns himself; nothing is done to him by another hand within the frame of the image — and this treatise's comparative method works best, across every part that follows, when it stays close to that specific structural feature rather than reaching for surrealist material whose organising logic departs from it. The asymmetry first identified in Part II — male botanical madness staged as self-coronation, female botanical madness staged as something witnessed, painted, or given away — recurs here in a milder form, and this treatise does not wish to elide it in pursuit of a tidier synthesis: surrealism's vegetal imagination, like the wider visual tradition this treatise surveys, distributes its attention unevenly across that asymmetry rather than resolving it.

4.5  Toward a Working Definition

What can be extracted, with appropriate caution, from this survey is a working definition of what this treatise will call the surrealist-vegetal mode: an artistic operation in which botanical material is deployed not as ornament, symbol, or background but as a destabilizing agent of bodily and categorical identity, producing composite forms that are neither fully human nor fully vegetal, and that register, in the witnessing party, the same double affect of attraction and repulsion Edgar's "side-piercing sight" names in Shakespeare's scene. This mode, this treatise argues, did not require any direct knowledge of Lear's flower scene to develop — Dalí's stated theory of metamorphosis draws on Ovidian and psychoanalytic sources, not Shakespearean ones — but it converges with extraordinary precision on the same structural logic the Jacobean stage direction first encoded: the body's coronation by, and dissolution into, the vegetal world's own disordered abundance.

Self-organisation · diversity as engine

The surrealist method — the deliberate jamming-together of incompatible categories until something neither-one-nor-the-other emerges — is, in complexity terms, a technology for manufacturing exactly the high-diversity, high-intensity interaction the Santa Fe scientists found at the edge of chaos. Ernst's frottage, Dalí's composite plant-creatures: each is a two-person bench exercise conducted between materials, novelty emerging between the actors rather than from any single intending hand, the image arriving where no one can quite say it came from. Patricia Shaw's account of emergent meaning could caption any surrealist canvas — what seems solid melts at the edges while what seems shapeless gains form, at the same time. The boardroom that demands every element of its strategy be legible and aligned in advance has chosen the frozen lattice over the surreal thaw, and has thereby guaranteed that nothing it has not already imagined can ever emerge from it.

Novelty is what arrives when the categories are no longer kept apart.

4.6  Vegetable Man: Syd Barrett, Arcimboldo, and the Pop Song That Wore Its Own Dissolution

The surrealist-vegetal mode did not remain in the galleries. In August 1967, at the very peak of London's Summer of Love, a twenty-one-year-old Syd Barrett — founder, singer and chief songwriter of Pink Floyd, and a former art student at Camberwell — sat in his manager Peter Jenner's flat an hour before a recording session that needed a song, and wrote one by simply describing the clothes he was wearing, attaching a chorus that asked "Vegetable Man — where are you?" The result, recorded that same day and then suppressed for nearly fifty years as "too dark and disturbing" to release, is one of the most exact pop-cultural reappearances of Lear's flower-crowning the twentieth century produced — and, like Švankmajer's decomposing vegetable woman, it arrived with no need of any conscious debt to the play. Its title, by the songwriter's own account, derives directly from a painting this treatise's surrealist genealogy has been circling: Giuseppe Arcimboldo's Summer (1572/3), the composite portrait built entirely from fruit, vegetables and grain — a literal vegetable man — that Barrett had encountered as an art student, and to which he tipped a knowing wink by posing, in a period promotional photograph, with spring onions tied to his head. A pop musician crowning his own head with vegetables, at the threshold of a mental breakdown, in conscious homage to a sixteenth-century composite-vegetal portrait: the floral derangement of the sovereign, transposed into the idiom of psychedelic rock and the iconography of the pop star as the new sovereign of the cultural moment.

What makes the song a genuinely radical addition to this treatise's argument, rather than a charming coincidence, is the precise way it inverts the central gesture of Lear's flower scene while arriving at the same destination. Lear, on the heath, tears the clothes off — "Off, off, you lendings!" — stripping the borrowed coverings of art and rank to reach the "thing itself," the "poor, bare, forked animal" beneath. Barrett does the exact opposite: he piles the clothes on, and discovers that there is nothing underneath them at all. The song is a near-complete inventory of garments — yellow shoes, blue velvet trousers, paisley shirt, turquoise waistcoat, nylon socks bought in a box, a black watch "with the date in a little hole" — and it culminates in a line of devastating ontological flatness: "And all the lot is what I got / It's what I wear, it's what you see / It must be me, it's what I am, / Vegetable Man." Where Lear's tragedy is the discovery that beneath the lendings there is a naked, suffering, irreducible human truth, Barrett's is the discovery that beneath the lendings there is only more lendings — that the self has become wholly exhausted by its surfaces, a man identical with his clothes, a vegetable assembled, like Arcimboldo's, entirely from its purchasable parts. The two are the same study of the self at the edge of dissolution, approached from opposite directions: Lear strips to find the irreducible core and finds unaccommodated man; Barrett accumulates to find the core and finds it absent, the "vegetable man" who is only "what you see." Jenner's description of the recording is itself unimprovable as a gloss on Lear's exposure: it is, he said, "like psychological flashing" — the naked, breaking mind involuntarily exposed, which is precisely what Lear means by "Take physic, pomp; / Expose thyself to feel what wretches feel."

The suppressed song · what the band could not let out of the room

"Vegetable Man" was recorded and then buried. The master tapes bore the handwritten instruction "not to be used for LP"; Roger Waters took it off the album because, in Jenner's words, "it was too dark, and it is"; it circulated only as a bootleg for half a century before an official release in 2016. This treatise has already traced, in its account of Nahum Tate's century-long suppression of King Lear's ending (Part XIV), the way a culture's institutions move to suppress the image of sovereign dissolution that frightens them most — and the burial of "Vegetable Man" is the same gesture performed by a rock band upon its own founder. The song was suppressed for precisely the reason Lear's un-redeemed ending was suppressed: it shows the dissolution without the consolation, the breakdown without the recovery, the vegetable man without the restored king. And there is a sharper organisational point folded into the suppression. Barrett, in this period, was the shadow system of his own band made flesh — the destabilising, off-protocol, increasingly ungovernable source of exactly the creative novelty (the edge-of-chaos genius) that had made the band, and that the band could no longer contain. His bandmates' response was the response of every legitimate system to a shadow system grown too disruptive to manage: they suppressed the output and, within months, replaced the man. Critics have repeatedly heard in the song's raw, flat, declarative nihilism a prefiguration of punk a decade early — "Syd Barrett inventing punk right here," the maverick whose breakdown at the edge produced a form the centre would not legitimate until the fringe had caught up with it. The band buried the vegetable man for the same reason Tate buried the dead Cordelia: a culture will stage the flower-crowned dissolution only when it can promise a recovery, and will suppress the version — Lear's, Barrett's — that offers the edge with no way back.

Every institution buries its vegetable man — the dissolution shown without the consolation — and calls the burial taste; but the suppressed song is where the edge told its truth.

Set beside the complexity theorists, the song reads as a casualty report from the edge of chaos — the necessary dark companion to the surrealists' playful composite-creatures and the management literature's optimistic talk of "creativity flourishing at the edge." For the edge of chaos, this treatise has insisted throughout, is not a safe place; it is the narrow, productive, terrifying band where a system's old order has dissolved and a new one has not yet formed, and McMillan's own warning — that beyond the edge lies Kauffman's "steam," the total dissolution in which no pattern can hold — is exactly the territory "Vegetable Man" reports from. Barrett is the artist who went to the edge for the novelty and could not get back: the same passage that, productively traversed, yields The Piper at the Gates of Dawn's visionary invention yields, six months and an ocean of LSD later, the man who has "been looking all over the place for a place for me / But it ain't anywhere, it just ain't anywhere" — the unaccommodated man of psychedelic London, homeless in exactly Lear's sense, the sovereign of the cultural moment dissolved into the vegetal flatness of his own surfaces. Peter Jenner's verdict on the discarded songs of this period was that "they're like Van Gogh" — art purchased at the price of the maker's disintegration. It is the oldest and most uncomfortable knowledge about the edge, the knowledge the corn-king ritual encodes and the management consultants prefer to forget: that the place where new order is born is the same place where selves are lost, and that the flower-crown sits on the head of the one who has gone there on everyone else's behalf. The edge that yields the vision and the edge that yields the breakdown are the same edge; Lear's weed-crown and Barrett's spring onions are worn by the same figure — the one sent to the threshold of dissolution to bring back what only dissolution can produce, who does not always come back.

Complexity Coda · IV

The surrealist-vegetal mode this part defined treats metamorphosis as a destabilising agent of bodily and categorical identity — composite forms neither fully one thing nor fully another, produced not by design but by the artist's deliberate refusal to keep categories separate. This is, descriptively, an uncannily exact account of what Stacey calls the edge of chaos: a zone in which old categorical boundaries (manager and managed, plan and execution, the organisation's official structure and its actual lived practice) loosen without yet resolving into any new, equally stable arrangement. Most change programmes try to design their way straight through this zone as quickly as possible, treating the in-between state as a regrettable transition cost rather than as the period in which any genuinely new organisational form actually gets made. Dalí's stated theory of metamorphosis assumed the destabilised, composite state was where the interesting work happened, not an embarrassment to be minimised; an edge-of-chaos-literate change practice makes the identical wager about the organisation's own destabilised middle period.

Stacey's distinction between Type 1 and Type 2 abstraction supplies a useful diagnostic for separating the surrealist-vegetal mode's two registers from one another. Dalí's stated theory of metamorphosis, however idiosyncratic its Ovidian and psychoanalytic sources, remains close to experience in Stacey's sense — a Type 1 abstraction, generalising from the felt, embodied strangeness of forms refusing to stay categorically separate, without claiming the generalisation exhausts what any single canvas is doing. A change-management framework that imports "transformation" or "metamorphosis" as a slide-deck metaphor, detached from any actual local interaction it claims to describe, performs the opposite move: a Type 2 abstraction borrowing surrealism's vocabulary of destabilised form while discarding the close, patient attention to actual experience that made the vocabulary meaningful in the first place. The edge-of-chaos zone this treatise's prior coda located in the surrealist-vegetal mode is genuinely productive only for an observer still occupying something like Dalí's Type 1 relationship to it — immersed enough in the actual destabilisation to learn from its specific, ungeneralisable texture, not merely naming it from a detached, already-abstracted distance.

V. Three Screens, Three Climates: Kozintsev, Brook, and Kurosawa Stage the Unstageable

Blow, winds, and crack your cheeks! Rage! Blow!
You cataracts and hurricanoes, spout
Till you have drench’d our steeples, drown’d the cocks!— King Lear, III.ii — the storm the camera must find

The three canonical film adaptations of King Lear — Grigori Kozintsev's Korol Lir (1971), Peter Brook's King Lear (1971), and Akira Kurosawa's Ran (1985, an unacknowledged-until-late but eventually explicit transposition) — arrived within a fourteen-year window of each other and constitute, taken together, a kind of natural experiment in how radically the same textual seed can flower (the verb is, in this context, unavoidable) into opposed visual climates. This part argues that each director's solution to the flower-crowning problem — a moment of pure stage spectacle that resists straightforward cinematic translation — reveals as much about each director's underlying theory of madness, sovereignty, and nature as any explicit critical statement could.

5.1  Kozintsev's Russian Steppe: Madness as Communal Fate

Kozintsev's film, scripted from a screenplay by Boris Pasternak and scored by Dmitri Shostakovich, relocates Lear's Britain to a landscape the critic Jan Kott — whose theoretical importance to this entire treatise will be addressed fully in Part VIII — described in terms that emphasize collectivity over individual psychology. Kott's own account, published in the New York Review of Books, recalls the film's hovel scene with an intensity that exceeds ordinary film criticism: the abandoned hovel where the exiled old man who was once king finds his last shelter teems with human bodies. We see old men and women, cripples shaking their stubby limbs, women nursing infants, and even a yurodivy ("God's fool"), intoning unending litanies which mix with "poor Tom's" exorcisms. Kozintsev's interpretive choice, on this account, is to dissolve Lear's individual madness into a landscape already saturated with collective Russian suffering — the exiles from the royal court walk past "refugees" streaming into the steppe from razed settlements and villages — such that the king's flower-crowned derangement becomes legible not as a unique personal catastrophe but as one instance among many of a shared national affliction.

Critic Alexander Anikst's praise of the film, recorded in Wikipedia's survey of the play's stage and screen history, frames this choice explicitly against what Anikst takes to be a rival, more sensationalist approach: no attempts at sensationalism, no efforts to "modernise" Shakespeare by introducing Freudian themes, Existentialist ideas, eroticism, or sexual perversion. [Kozintsev] has simply made a film of Shakespeare's tragedy. The PBS retrospective on the major Lear films extends this account, noting that Kozintsev's choice of the diminutive actor Jüri Järvet — unlike Lears such as Scofield and Welles, he does not fill out the screen by himself — was deliberate: the film thus replaces what may be majesty in other versions with the earth itself. In Kozintsev's vision, the flower-crowned image's mythic substrate (Part III's corn-king material) is foregrounded rather than psychologized: it is the land's own fertility-cycle, the land's own population of refugees and fools, that the king's madness comes to represent, rather than one man's private breakdown.

5.2  Brook's Bleached Jutland: Madness as Absurdist Void

Peter Brook's film, shot on the same approximate timeline but to radically different visual ends, removes precisely the collective social texture Kozintsev supplies. Explicitly indebted to Kott's "King Lear or Endgame" essay (treated in Part VIII), Brook's film was, per Wikipedia's account, inspired by the absurdist theatre of playwrights such as Samuel Beckett and upon release was noted for its bleak tone and wintry atmosphere. The PBS retrospective characterizes the resulting tonal signature in unforgiving terms — it is only available in DVD in UK PAL format, the writer notes almost as an aside, before getting to the substance: the film is shot in black and white, with an emphasis on black, vast landscapes, snow, creaking old castles, and takes great liberties with the plot, omitting whole scenes and moving things around to suit Brook's dramatic intentions. Pauline Kael's review, already quoted in Part III, named the result Night of the Living Dead; Robert Hatch's contrasting assessment in The Nation, also recorded by Wikipedia, called it as excellent a filming of the play as one can expect; Vincent Canby in the New York Times called it an exalting Lear, full of exquisite terror.

What unites these wildly opposed critical receptions is agreement on the film's affective extremity, an extremity for which the flower-crowning moment — which Brook's stripped, near-empty visual grammar renders with minimal pictorial elaboration, no riot of botanical color, no Millais-style profusion — becomes almost a test case. Where Kozintsev's flowers (and his entire mise-en-scène) are populated, textured, communal, Brook's are isolated, severe, nearly abstract: two tiny figures, as the scholar Roger Warren's description (recorded by Wikipedia) of Brook's earlier 1962 Stratford staging puts it, conveying both the human pathos … and the universal scale … of the scene against a huge, empty white stage. Brook's governing aesthetic decision, consistent across stage and film versions separated by nine years, is to let absence do the work that Kozintsev's populated landscape does through plenitude. The corn-king's failed ritual (Part III) finds its bleakest possible visual correlative here: a wasteland literally emptied of the harvest's communal witnesses.

5.3  Kurosawa's Color: Madness as Cosmic Spectacle

Ran inverts both preceding strategies. Where Kozintsev offers populated grey and Brook offers depopulated grey, Kurosawa offers, per Wikipedia's account, vibrant colour: external scenes in yellows, blues and greens, interiors in browns and ambers, complemented by costume designer Emi Wada's Oscar-winning colour-coded costumes for each family member's soldiers. Jan Kott's later essay on the film, "The Edo Lear," published after both Brook's and Kozintsev's films had already established the genre's grim visual baseline, registers something close to relief at Kurosawa's departure from that baseline, while still situating the Japanese director's choices within the same comparative framework: Brook's Jutland and Kozintsev's broad Russian steppe were a way of introducing Lear into universal history. Kurosawa places the drama of Lear in sixteenth-century Japan.

The displacement of the source text's specific botanical imagery (Lear's literal flower-crowning has no direct equivalent in Ran's plot, since Kurosawa's Hidetora figure does not undergo an identical scene) is itself revealing: Kurosawa retains the play's structural logic of vegetal/natural sovereignty — Letterboxd reviews of the film repeatedly emphasize that the mountains at the background, the flowers, the clouds are rendered with such compositional precision that every frame could be a painting — while relocating the specific device of bodily flower-coronation into a more diffuse, environment-wide aestheticization of nature's relationship to political collapse. One Letterboxd reviewer's offhand comment that the film maintains a certain degree of harmony… regardless of what is depicted on screen, be it war, manipulation, betrayal, or massacre identifies precisely the visual strategy this treatise has been tracking: nature's beauty and order persisting, even intensifying, exactly at the moment human sovereignty disintegrates most completely — the corn-king's land outliving, gorgeously, the corn-king's own failed ritual.

5.4  A Note on the 1953 Welles Broadcast and the Eccentricity Thesis

The PBS retrospective makes one further observation worth preserving here, concerning the broader history of Lear adaptation prior to the 1971 cluster: the first three film versions of the play taken historically are the most eccentric — Orson Welles in 1953, Paul Scofield in 1971, and Kozintsev's Russian version of the same year, with the versions after this trio having hewed to a more traditional line. This is a useful corrective against any temptation to read the flower-crowning image's screen history as a smooth, continuous evolution toward ever-greater fidelity or ever-greater abstraction. The eccentricity, this treatise suggests, is in fact concentrated precisely where the source text's own image is most resistant to cinematic naturalism — the flower-crowned king is, after all, a piece of pure theatrical convention, legible instantly on a stage where an actor can simply walk on wearing a garland, and legible only with much greater interpretive labor on a screen, where the apparatus of cinematic realism (lighting continuity, costume verisimilitude, the camera's relentless specificity) makes "fantastical" dress either a literal costuming problem to be solved naturalistically or an invitation to the kind of stylization Brook, Kozintsev, and Kurosawa each pursued by entirely separate routes.

Complexity Coda · V

Kozintsev's collectivised Lear replaces individual majesty with the land's own population of refugees and fools; Kurosawa transposes the heath onto the Sengoku battlefield's total social collapse. Both directorial choices read Lear's personal undoing as inseparable from a wider systemic one, and this is precisely the methodological move complex responsive processes asks of anyone analysing organisational change: that the individual leader's crisis never be treated in isolation from the web of relationships and local interactions actually constituting the system around them. A change initiative that locates the entire drama in the departing or arriving executive — the corn-king model the previous part's coda criticised — repeats the error Brook's more psychologically intimate staging risks and Kozintsev's wide-screen, populated heath corrects: it mistakes a single figure's story for the whole system's story, when the whole system, like Kozintsev's refugees, was already moving long before and will keep moving long after that figure's personal scene has ended.

Figuration · the system, not the sovereign

Kozintsev fills the screen with the dispossessed because he intuited what Elias would insist on and Shaw would build a practice around: that the unit of the drama is never the isolated sovereign but the whole figuration of interdependent people whose shifting balances of power constitute the only reality there is. Lear alone is a psychological case; Lear-in-the-figuration — refugees streaming across the steppe, the kingdom's whole web of relating reorganising around his fall — is a study in self-organising change. This is the same correction the emergent-change literature makes against the heroic-leader story: change, Aitken and Higgs found in their study of a hundred change narratives, “can start anywhere in the system,” and most often starts at the periphery, the front line, the edge where the organism meets its world — which is to say it starts among Kozintsev's refugees, not in the throne room the camera keeps leaving behind.

The throne is never the protagonist; the figuration is.

Elias's figurational sociology was developed, not incidentally, partly through his own historical studies of court society — the dense, intricately ranked, perpetually anxious figuration surrounding an absolute monarch, in which every gesture's meaning depends on a constantly shifting calculus of precedence Elias traces in granular historical detail. Kozintsev's collectivised reading of Lear, populating the screen with refugees and fools rather than isolating the king's psychology, is in effect an Eliasian rather than a purely psychological reading before complex responsive processes theory existed to name it as such: the king's fall is legible only as a shift in the entire court figuration's power-ratios, not as an isolable individual event. Change programmes that locate an organisational crisis entirely within a departing executive's individual failings, rather than within the wider figuration's shifting balance of interdependence that both produced and outlived that executive's tenure, are choosing Brook's more intimate, individually-centred frame when the situation, like Kozintsev's wide screen, actually requires the figurational one.

Complexity Coda · V

Three directors filmed the same heath and reached, by three routes, one finding the change literature keeps rediscovering: that the storm cannot be staged, only released. Kozintsev flooded his frame with displaced peasants, Brook stripped the screen to Beckettian emptiness, Kurosawa translated the heath into the Sengoku battlefield — and each, refusing to let the camera impose a single controlling order on the scene, let meaning emerge from the collision of elements, exactly as Patricia Shaw insists change emerges from the uncontrolled gathering rather than the directed plan. The flower-crown survives all three translations because it is not a costume detail but the visible sign of the same law in every medium: when the gardener-king stops dictating the frame, the field composes itself, and the composition is truer than any the director could have decreed.

VI. The Standing Chord: Reimann's Lear and the Operatic Translation of Unreason

Music drama's relationship to Lear's flower-crowned madness is structured by a strange historical lacuna this treatise must first acknowledge before examining the work that eventually filled it. For most of the operatic repertoire's existence — a repertoire that found room for Verdi's Macbeth, Otello, and Falstaff, and for endless settings of Romeo and Juliet — King Lear remained operatically almost untouched. The reason, as this part will argue, is structural: opera's nineteenth-century grammar of madness (the coloratura mad scene, exemplified by Lucia di Lammermoor, Ophélie in Ambroise Thomas's Hamlet, and a dozen other "document in madness" showpieces built for soprano voice) was built for exactly the gendered, elegiac, Ophelia-coded register Part II identified — and had no ready equivalent register for an old man's politically lucid, baritone-voiced ravings.

6.1  Verdi's Ghost

The opera most relevant to this treatise's argument is, paradoxically, the one that was never written. Reviewing two later Lear operas for a scholarly journal, one critic opens by naming the absence directly: of all the operas which never moved beyond the planning stage, Giuseppe Verdi's King Lear will probably head anyone's list as our greatest loss. Verdi's documented fascination with the play, sustained across decades of his career without ever issuing in a finished score, suggests that the same composer who found musical-dramatic solutions for Macbeth's hallucinations and Otello's jealousy-madness could not, in the end, find one for Lear's flower-crowned scene specifically — a negative data point this treatise reads as confirming, rather than merely illustrating, the operatic tradition's structural difficulty with this particular variety of unreason.
