The same review notes the curious historical filler that occupied the gap: except for a forgotten work performed in 1937 by an obscure Italian composer with close ties to the Fascist party, no one seems to have taken on Shakespeare's monumental tragedy until the last quarter of the 20th century — the Vito Frazzi Re Lear of 1939, with a libretto by Giovanni Papini, a work that has not entered the repertoire and exists now largely as a historical footnote to the absence it briefly interrupted.

6.2  Reimann, Fischer-Dieskau, and the Baritone Solution

The gap was decisively filled in 1978 by the German composer Aribert Reimann, whose Lear, with a libretto by Claus H. Henneberg after Johann Joachim Eschenburg's eighteenth-century German translation, was written specifically — the genesis story is recorded by multiple sources — at the urging of the baritone Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau, who according to Wikipedia had suggested the subject to the composer as early as 1968, a full decade before the eventual Munich premiere. The Paris Opera's program notes confirm the long gestation: approached at the end of the sixties by the baritone Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau, who dreamed of portraying the character immortalised by Shakespeare, Reimann agreed to take up the challenge for his third opera, with the libretto only begun in 1972 and the Munich commission not secured until 1975.

The choice of baritone rather than the more conventionally "mad-scene-coded" soprano or tenor registers is itself a significant departure from the operatic mad-scene tradition Part 6.1 outlined above, and the resulting score's harmonic language confirms that Reimann was building something structurally new rather than adapting an existing operatic madness-vocabulary. Reimann's own diary description of the opera's final bars, preserved in Schott Music's promotional materials, is worth quoting for its sheer technical specificity: the standing chord begins to slowly vibrate from the bottom, like an earthquake, a sonic image of geological rather than vocal-coloratura disturbance. The contemporary review by Wolfgang Schreiber in the Süddeutsche Zeitung, also preserved by Schott, describes the broader sound-world in equally non-traditional terms: tone clusters of every intensity, quarter-tone frictions, several minutes of standing and turning sound surfaces, brass clusters of monstrous harshness, confusing rhythmic shifts, lyrical pausing of solo parts — these tonal means are applied for sharp characterization … never as mere material demonstration.

6.3  The Mad Scene Without Coloratura

A review of a 2014 Hamburg State Opera production, preserved in Project MUSE's journal database, makes the crucial comparative judgment this treatise has been building toward: Lear himself fits into both worlds but his final mad scene is as heartbreaking as anything in opera, achieved by the baritone Bo Skovhus, of whom the same review notes he is one of the top singing actors of our time, in full command of the role. The Paris Opera's later promotional copy for a Calixto Bieito production elaborates on what this baritone-coded mad scene accomplishes differently from the soprano tradition: Skovhus, the copy states, interpreting the role with skin-deep sensitivity, … turns the distant crownless king into a poignant reminder of our human condition — note the specific word crownless, which this treatise's entire argument has been organized around: the opera's mad scene stages not a crown of flowers exactly (operatic staging of this specific image varies production to production, and Reimann's libretto, working from a German prose tradition rather than Shakespeare's verse directly, does not require literal floral costuming) but the same underlying logic of sovereignty stripped to its bodily and vocal residue.

6.4  Staging Variations: From Ponnelle to Kosky

The opera's staging history offers a useful coda to Part V's cinematic survey, because it shows directors confronting the identical translation-problem — how to render Lear's vegetal madness in a non-naturalistic medium — across an even wider range of solutions than film permitted. The original 1978 production, designed by Jean-Pierre Ponnelle with costumes by Pet Halmen, established a visual baseline against which subsequent directors have consistently defined themselves by departure. A 2008 Frankfurt revival, reviewed at length by the blog Ionarts, describes a deliberately anti-spectacular design choice for the mad scene specifically: an intriguing setting for the mad scene, indeed — although it might have been more impressive if the upper reaches of Mount Trash, which Edgar-cum-Tom climbed and descended from, hadn't wobbled like a lambs' tail — a "Mount Trash" set piece that converts the play's cornfield/heath imagery into a literal mountain of refuse, an unsubtle but structurally faithful translation of Part III's "idle weeds" logic into stage-design vocabulary.

Most recently, Barrie Kosky's production for the Komische Oper Berlin, staged at Tempelhof Airport according to the company's own program description, frames the entire opera's emotional argument in terms that compress this treatise's central concern into a single sentence: Barrie Kosky stages this classic of modern music theatre as the endgame of a king who knows how little time he has left, yet refuses to hear the truth. A man who is loved, but must die alone. The word endgame here is almost certainly a deliberate citation of Kott's "King Lear or Endgame" essay (Part VIII), confirming that Reimann's operatic tradition has, across nearly five decades of staging history, remained in continuous dialogue with the absurdist critical framework that shaped the cinematic adaptations examined in Part V.

Complexity Coda · VI

Reimann's standing chord — the orchestral device by which the baritone's collapse is rendered as a sustained, almost geological vibration rather than a single dramatic event — supplies an unusually precise musical analogue for what Prigogine's dissipative-structures physics calls a system held far from equilibrium: not a single collapse-and-recovery but a sustained, energetically costly holding-open of instability, in which order and disorder coexist for an extended duration rather than resolving cleanly into one or the other. Most organisational change models are built, like a conventional cadence, to resolve: tension introduced, tension released, the chord allowed to come to rest in the home key of the new status quo. Stacey's edge-of-chaos literature suggests the more honest musical analogue for sustained organisational transformation is closer to Reimann's standing chord than to a cadence — a long, unresolved, energetically demanding vibration that the system simply learns to live inside, rather than a dissonance briefly tolerated en route to a guaranteed resolution.

Streatfield's paradox of control finds its clearest musical equivalent in Reimann's standing chord, sustained rather than resolved across the scene's duration. Streatfield's own account of his experience managing major logistics transformation insists that the manager who claims, even sincerely, to be fully in control of a complex change process is occupying a fiction Lear's own first scene already occupies and the heath subsequently strips away; the manager who claims total absence of control, by contrast, abandons the very participation Stacey insists remains both possible and necessary throughout. The baritone holding Reimann's standing chord is neither resolving the dissonance into false consonance nor abandoning the note altogether; he is doing the vocally and dramatically demanding work of sustaining an unresolved tension for as long as the dramatic truth requires it, which is, in Streatfield's vocabulary, exactly what paradoxical control actually asks of anyone leading through a genuine, unresolved organisational instability.

Opera could not score the heath until it abandoned the key; some collapses can be sung only by music that has given up the law of harmony.
Complexity Coda · VI

Reimann could score Lear’s heath only by abandoning the key — by writing music that surrenders the central organising authority of tonality and lets sound self-organise at the edge of noise. This is the operatic form of the treatise’s whole thesis: tonality is the machine metaphor of music, a hierarchy in which every note traces back to a governing centre; atonality is the flux, order emerging horizontally from the relations between notes with no sovereign key to command them. Verdi spent a lifetime unable to write his Lear because the scene demanded a music he did not yet have the language for — the music of a sovereignty dissolved. The flower-crown and the twelve-tone row are the same discovery: that the richest order is the one no central authority imposes, the pattern that blooms when the key, like the king, has abdicated.

VII. The Body of Darkness: Butoh and the Choreography of Vegetal Collapse

Thou art the thing itself.— King Lear, III.iv — the body reduced to its bare animal fact

Of all the performance traditions this treatise surveys, Japanese Butoh — ankoku butō, "the dance of utter darkness" — stands furthest from any direct line of influence from Shakespeare's text, and is included here precisely because its independent development converges so exactly on the structural logic this treatise has been tracking that the convergence becomes, in itself, the strongest available evidence that the flower-crowned Lear names something close to a cultural universal rather than a merely local Jacobean theatrical convention.

7.1  Origins in Catastrophe

Butoh's founders, Tatsumi Hijikata and Kazuo Ohno, developed the form in the direct aftermath of a national catastrophe whose scale dwarfs anything Lear's Britain undergoes, but whose structural relationship to bodily and political collapse is comparable. Wikipedia's account places the form's origin precisely: following World War II, butoh arose in 1959 through collaborations between its two key founders, in a milieu the Tate Papers scholarly essay describes as shaped by direct personal loss — the personal effects of the war on Hijikata cannot be underestimated — he claimed that three of his older brothers were killed in action — and by the broader cultural trauma the form's chroniclers consistently invoke. The mystical-ritual website Shadow Work Performance states the connection in its most condensed form: butoh was born from the ashes of Hiroshima and Nagasaki, a deliberate radical rejection of Western dance aesthetics and a descent into the darkest territories of human experience.

This origin in collective catastrophe rather than individual psychological case-history aligns Butoh more closely with Kozintsev's collectivized reading of Lear (Part V) than with any individually psychologized account of madness, and it is worth noting at the outset that Butoh's relationship to vegetal and natural imagery, like Lear's own, emerges from rather than escapes this catastrophic ground. The form does not offer vegetation as pastoral consolation for political ruin; it offers vegetation, like ash, like corpse-flesh, as one of several states the post-catastrophic body can be made to pass through.

7.2  The White Body and Its Vegetal Kin

The visual signature of Butoh performance — Wikipedia's summary describes full body paint (white or dark or gold), near or complete nudity, shaved heads, grotesque costumes, clawed hands, rolled-up eyes and mouths opened in silent screams — places the dancer's body in a liminal state between several categories of being simultaneously: corpse, infant, animal, mineral, and, crucially for this treatise's argument, vegetal matter. Shadow Work Performance's account of the form's iconic image elaborates the corpse-association at length, but the same account's broader catalogue of states the Butoh body is trained to inhabit explicitly includes primordial consciousness: pre-human, pre-civilized states — the body as animal, insect, mineral, void — a taxonomy that, while not naming "plant" as a discrete category, structurally requires it: a body moving through every pre-human category of being cannot skip the vegetal kingdom, and Butoh practice and choreographic notation (butoh-fu, discussed below) confirms that it does not.

Hijikata's own choreographic vocabulary made this explicit. Wikipedia's biographical account notes that he explored the transmutation of the human body into other forms, such as those of animals and developed a poetic and surreal choreographic language, butoh-fu — fu means "word" in Japanese — to help the dancer transform into other states of being. The italicized word "transmutation" here is doing exactly the work Dalí's stated theory of metamorphosis (Part IV) does for surrealist painting: both traditions, entirely independent of one another, arrive at a vocabulary of bodily transformation into non-human, frequently vegetal, states as the central technical and philosophical project of their respective art forms.

7.3  Hijikata's Surrealist Debts, Made Explicit

Remarkably, the connection between Butoh and the surrealist-vegetal tradition surveyed in Part IV is not merely structural-analogical but directly documented. Wikipedia's account of Hijikata's career notes that his 1968 solo work, Hijikata Tatsumi and Japanese People: Revolt of the Body, drew on a continental literary-surrealist genealogy that included Lautréamont, Artaud, and Genet, alongside Yukio Mishima's own theatre of the body, situating Butoh's vegetal-bodily transformations within a European avant-garde lineage rather than purely indigenous Japanese sources, even as the form's performance vocabulary remained distinctively rooted in Japanese theatrical and folk-religious traditions (noh's ghost-logic, the figure of the yurodivy-adjacent wandering holy fool that Kozintsev's film, discussed in Part V, also drew upon from within the Russian tradition).

7.4  Death Surrounded by Flowers: Hijikata's Own Coda

The single most directly resonant piece of evidence this treatise has located linking Butoh to the specific flower-and-mortality complex this entire project investigates concerns not a performance but Hijikata's own death and its photographic documentation. The literary magazine 3:AM's account, drawing on Stephen Barber's biography of the choreographer, records that before Hijikata's body was cremated, one of his friends, the photographer Eikoh Hosoe, recorded an image of Hijikata in his coffin, surrounded by bunches of flowers, his face in death described as aberrantly tranquil, his mouth open, his waist-long hair combed back from his forehead. The same account preserves one of Hijikata's own late statements about his choreographic project, made in the year before his death: I would like to have a person, who has already died, die over and over inside my body … I may not know death, but it knows me.

This image — a body, ringed in flowers, that has spent its entire artistic career rehearsing death and transformation into pre-human states — is not a citation of Lear's flower-crowning in any direct sense; there is no evidence Hosoe or Hijikata had Shakespeare's stage direction in mind. But the structural identity is exact: a body marked by flowers at the precise moment its claim to ordinary, continuous, sovereign selfhood (Hijikata as the living director of his own company, Lear as "the king himself") has been most fully surrendered. The flowers arrive, in both cases, not to console the living figure but to mark, publicly and visibly, the threshold the figure has crossed — and in both cases the witnesses present (Hosoe's camera; Edgar's "side-piercing sight") register that threshold-crossing as something that demands to be looked at and recorded, however unbearable the looking.

7.5  Sankai Juku and the Risk of the Form

One further detail from Butoh's international touring history deserves inclusion here as a sobering coda to this part's argument. Wikipedia's account of the touring company Sankai Juku records that during one performance … in which the performers hung upside down from ropes from a tall building in Seattle, one of the ropes broke, resulting in the death of a performer. The form's commitment to staging the body at its absolute physical limit — the same commitment that produces its vegetal-transformation imagery — carries, unlike Dalí's painted canvases or Reimann's musical score, a genuine and realized physical risk to the performing body. This is a register of literalized danger that none of this treatise's other case studies quite reach, and it stands as a reminder that the flower-crowned Lear, however much cultural elaboration this treatise traces outward from it, began as an image of a body in genuine, embodied crisis — cold, exposed, exhausted, "cut to the brains" — before it ever became available for any later artist's aesthetic refinement.

Complexity Coda · VII

Butoh developed, this part showed, from collective catastrophe rather than individual case-history, and its vegetal imagery offers the post-catastrophic body a set of states to pass through rather than a destination to arrive at. This is a useful corrective to change-management language that still speaks of transformation as a linear passage from a defined "current state" to a defined "future state," with the implicit promise that the passage, once completed, is finished. Hijikata's butoh-fu notation trains the dancer to inhabit successive non-human states — animal, mineral, vegetal, void — without any single state being final; the choreography's discipline lies in the quality of each transition, not in the achievement of a last, settled form. An organisation that has genuinely been through transformative change rarely arrives at a settled future state either, however much the closing slide of the consultant's deck implies otherwise; what it has actually acquired, on Stacey and Mowles's account, is a changed capacity for passing through its next unscripted instability — which is to say, a changed relationship to the edge of chaos itself, not an exit from it.

Mead's pragmatist account of the self as constituted through gesture and anticipated response supplies an instructive frame for Hijikata's own butoh-fu notation, in which the dancer is trained to anticipate and embody the next state's gesture before fully inhabiting the present one — a continuous, forward-leaning process of self-formation through movement that Mead would recognise as close kin to his account of how the social self comes into being, moment to moment, through the gesture-response loop. An organisation passing through its own post-catastrophic period — the equivalent, in Stacey's vocabulary, of bounded instability rather than either rigid pre-crisis order or unbounded collapse — undergoes something structurally similar: it does not simply wait passively for a new stable form to arrive from outside, but continuously gestures toward possible next states, anticipating and responding to its own emerging pattern in real time, exactly as Hijikata's dancer gestures toward the next non-human state before fully arriving at it. The discipline lies, in both cases, in the quality of the continuous transition, not in the premature naming of an end-state no one inside the process can yet actually specify.

Butoh dances the body the throne forgot — the slow vegetal collapse in which the sovereign and the soil at last turn out to be the same matter.
Complexity Coda · VII

Butoh dances what the change literature can only describe: the body relinquishing its trained, controlled, vertical form and discovering the slow vegetal collapse into a different order entirely. The butoh body is a body at the edge of chaos — neither the rigid disciplined form of classical dance (the frozen court) nor formless thrashing (the killing storm), but the trembling, emergent, far-from-equilibrium state between, where new movement self-organises moment by moment. This is precisely Stacey’s living present: form arising continuously from the body’s local responses rather than from a choreography imposed in advance. Lear’s flower-crowned body, lowering itself among the weeds, is a butoh body — the sovereign form dissolving into the vegetal, and finding in the dissolution a truer order than the throne ever held.

VIII. Kott's Grotesque: The Theoretical Hinge Between Tragedy and the Absurd

The world of King Lear, like the world of the great Renaissance tragedies, is unredeemed… The Shakespearean Grotesque represents the world as a great mechanism, an empty machine.— Jan Kott, Shakespeare Our Contemporary

This treatise has invoked Jan Kott's 1964 essay "King Lear or Endgame" repeatedly across Parts III, V, and VI without yet pausing to examine its argument on its own terms. That examination is overdue, because Kott's essay functions as the single most consequential piece of mid-twentieth-century criticism for this treatise's entire subject: it is the text that gave directors and artists across multiple media — theatre, film, opera — explicit critical permission, and a explicit critical vocabulary, to treat Lear's flower-crowned madness not as Romantic pathos but as a species of the post-war theatrical grotesque.

8.1  Tragedy Re-Written as Grotesque

Kott's central terminological move is to distinguish sharply between tragedy and the grotesque as competing structures for organizing unbearable experience, and to argue that King Lear, properly read, belongs to the second category rather than the first. Semantic Scholar's summary of the essay's argument preserves Kott's key formulations: in the "new theatre" of Beckett, Dürrenmatt, and Ionesco, tragedy had been replaced by the grotesque: "Grotesque means tragedy re-written in different terms"; crucially, in the grotesque mode, the hero still loses his "struggle against the absolute," an absolute that is confirmed — the difference from classical tragedy lying not in the outcome (defeat remains defeat) but in the absence of any compensating dignity, meaning, or cosmic order that classical tragedy's catharsis depends upon.

A teaching resource prepared for Royal Holloway's English department distills the essay's central distinction with unusual clarity: tragedy is the theatre of priests, grotesque is the theatre of clowns, Kott writes, and the same resource explains the mechanism by which a scene tips from one register into the other: the tragic situation becomes grotesque when both alternatives of the choice imposed are absurd, irrelevant, or compromising. Applied to the flower-crowning scene specifically, this distinction does enormous interpretive work. A purely tragic reading of Lear's madness would require the scene to retain some recoverable dignity — madness as the price of insight, suffering as the precondition of wisdom, the Aristotelian economy in which the protagonist's fall purchases something of value for the audience's catharsis. Kott's grotesque reading instead insists that the scene offers no such recoverable value: the political insight Lear delivers ("a dog's obeyed in office") is real, but it changes nothing, saves no one, and is delivered by a man dressed, absurdly, as a field's own refuse. The insight and the indignity do not resolve into tragic meaning; they simply coexist, irreconcilably, which is precisely Kott's definition of the grotesque.

8.2  The Beckett Analogy and Its Textual Warrant

Kott's essay's title performs its argument before any sentence of the body text does, by yoking Lear directly to Samuel Beckett's Endgame (1957), first performed only a few years before Kott was writing. The PDF transcription of the original essay preserved by the document-sharing service PDF4PRO captures Kott's opening epigraphic juxtaposition with full force: he sets Lear's exchange with his Fool — King Lear. Dost thou call me fool, boy? / Fool. All thy other titles thou hast given away; that thou wast born with — directly against a line from Beckett: We are all born mad. Some remain so (Waiting for Godot, not Endgame, in this particular citation, though the broader essay's argument ranges across Beckett's work as a whole). The yoking is not casual; Kott's broader point, as Marguerite Tassi's scholarly retrospective on the essay's influence summarizes, is that Beckett's universe of the grotesque, of incomprehensible punishment and painful endurance, offered Kott the vision he needed to reinvent Shakespeare for Eastern European audiences who had undergone the atrocities of world war, concentration camps, fascism, and widespread oppression.

This biographical-historical grounding matters for understanding why Kott's essay, rather than any of the many prior centuries of Lear criticism, became the specific catalyst for the wave of bleak, stripped, absurdist Lear productions and films that followed it. Kott was not merely proposing a clever literary-critical analogy; he was proposing that post-war Eastern European audiences — his own audience, reading Shakespeare in a Poland that had lived through occupation, devastation, and the installation of a new totalitarian order on the ruins of the old — possessed direct experiential access to exactly the combination of political lucidity and complete practical helplessness that the flower-crowned Lear stages. Tassi's account confirms that Kott's stark modernist approach to Shakespeare's histories and tragedies … rejected both nineteenth-century romanticized interpretations and realist-historical stagings, replacing both with a third option this treatise has been calling, throughout, the grotesque mode.

8.3  Reception, Resistance, and Frank Kermode's Dissent

Kott's essay's influence was immediate and is well documented, but it did not go unchallenged, and the dissenting voices are worth preserving here for the sake of this treatise's own intellectual honesty. Frank Kermode's contemporary review in the New York Review of Books, while granting the essay's practical influence — Peter Hall quoted liberally from Kott in his notes on the Stratford version of the early history plays of Shakespeare; and Peter Brook's Lear, seen not long ago in New York, was avowedly indebted to Kott's essay on the play — registers sharp skepticism about the essay's scholarly soundness, particularly regarding Kott's treatment of A Midsummer Night's Dream elsewhere in the same volume: Kott the historian assumes that the Dream was written for a wedding, but Kott the Absurdist calls it cruel and scatological, a tonal whiplash Kermode treats as symptomatic of a broader interpretive overreach. Kermode's closing judgment is severe: he suggests that it is unusual for the speculations of a professor of literature to have so immediate an impact on the public stages, implying that theatrical practitioners' eagerness to stage Kott's reading reflects more about mid-century theatre's own appetites than about any demonstrated textual warrant in Shakespeare.

This treatise does not need to adjudicate definitively between Kott and Kermode to make use of the episode; what matters for the present argument is simply that the dispute happened, publicly, with major consequence for how the flower-crowned Lear was subsequently staged and filmed across the very productions examined in Parts V and VI. Whether or not Kott was "right" about Shakespeare's intentions, his essay demonstrably altered the available interpretive grammar within which Brook, and through Brook's influence many subsequent directors, approached the play's most theatrically resistant image.

8.4  The Grotesque as a General Theory of This Treatise's Subject

Stepping back from Kott's specific historical context, this treatise proposes that the tragedy/grotesque distinction he articulates offers the single most useful general theoretical frame for organizing the entire cross-media survey this document undertakes. Every case study examined so far — Millais's beautified Ophelia, Dalí's composite flower-creatures, Kozintsev's populated steppe, Brook's bleached absence, Reimann's standing chord, Hijikata's flower-ringed coffin — can be sorted along a spectrum running from attempted-tragic (Millais, arguably, in his determination to render Ophelia's death with full pictorial dignity and pathos) to fully grotesque (Brook, by explicit Kott-derived design; Ian Pollock's comics treatment, discussed in Part X, by the medium's own permission for visual exaggeration). The flower-crowned image's extraordinary cross-media durability, this treatise suggests in closing this part, derives precisely from its structural amenability to both readings simultaneously — it can be played for pathos, as the nineteenth century overwhelmingly chose to do with Ophelia, or it can be played for the grotesque, as the post-Kott twentieth century increasingly chose to do with Lear himself — and the fact that the same basic image (an aged or young figure, crowned or garlanded in vegetal material, witnessed by horrified onlookers) can sustain both readings without strain is, this treatise argues, exactly what has kept it generative across four centuries of reinterpretation.

8.5  Kott Read as an Organisational Theorist Before the Fact

It is worth observing, in closing this part, that Kott's central distinction between tragedy and the grotesque is not merely a piece of literary-critical vocabulary borrowed for convenience elsewhere in this treatise's argument, but is itself, examined closely, a theory of what happens when a system's official narrative about its own suffering and effort no longer matches the system's actual, lived experience of that suffering and effort. Tragedy, in Kott's account, requires a cosmic order capacious enough to make the hero's defeat meaningful — the suffering "pays for" something, redeems something, confirms some larger structure of value the audience can still believe in even as the particular hero falls. The grotesque, by contrast, names exactly the condition in which this redemptive cosmic order has quietly stopped being available, while the surface forms that once depended on it — the suffering, the eloquence, the dignity of bearing — continue, absurdly, to be performed anyway, by characters and by audiences alike, because no one has yet found, or been permitted to perform, the alternative. This is recognisably the same gap Chris Mowles's account of contemporary organisational life identifies between an organisation's espoused, tragedy-shaped narrative of its own change efforts — heroic resistance overcome, sacrifice rewarded, a redemptive arc the closing slide of any transformation programme's final review is all but contractually required to supply — and the considerably more grotesque, Kott-shaped texture of the actual, lived experience underneath that narrative, in which real effort and real suffering persist without any guarantee that either will be redeemed by the ending the official story has already, in advance, decided to tell. Kott did not know he was describing this gap when he wrote his 1964 essay on a Shakespearean tragedy newly legible to a post-war Polish audience; but the gap he names is the same gap Mowles's complex responsive processes literature spends its considerable critical energy trying to persuade contemporary management thinking to stop papering over.

Complexity Coda · VIII

Kott's reading of Lear as a Beckettian grotesque — tragedy curdled into something closer to the absurd, dignity and indignity inseparable within the same gesture — has its own organisational echo in Mowles's repeated insistence that paradox, not resolution, is the normal condition of organisational life. Planned change programmes are typically written in the register of tragedy resolved or comedy achieved: obstacles named, overcome, a happy or at least stable ending secured by the final review meeting. Kott's grotesque, and the complex responsive processes literature that in some ways inherits its refusal of tidy resolution, suggests organisational life is closer to the register Kott found in Lear and Beckett alike — dignity and indignity, control and helplessness, the official plan and its undignified actual unfolding, held together without either cancelling the other out.

Kermode's contemporary dissent from Kott — his charge that theatrical practitioners' eagerness to stage the Polish critic's reading reflected mid-century theatre's own appetites more than any demonstrated textual warrant — deserves its own organisational analogue, because change-management practice has its own recurring version of exactly this dispute: a persuasive theoretical framework, adopted with genuine and rapid enthusiasm across an entire field of practice, considerably outrunning the evidence available for its specific claims. Stacey's own career-long argument against what he called magico-mythical thinking in mainstream management literature is, in effect, change management's own Kermode moment, levelled not at a single critic's reading of a single play but at an entire industry's willingness to adopt frameworks — the seven habits, the two-by-two matrix, the eight-step model — whose persuasive theoretical elegance has, on Stacey's repeated argument, outrun what any rigorous study of actual organisational behaviour can support. This treatise does not need to adjudicate definitively between Stacey and his own critics, any more than it needed to adjudicate between Kott and Kermode, to note that the structure of the dispute — elegant theory, rapid practical adoption, slower and more contested scholarly verification — recurs with remarkable consistency across both fields.

The grotesque is the form truth takes when the old order has cracked but the new one has not yet set; it is the edge of chaos wearing a clown’s face.

IX. The Clinical Eye: Asylum Photography, Foucault, and the Costume of Diagnosis

The madman's voyage is at once a rigorous division and an absolute Passage… he is put in the interior of the exterior, and inversely.— Michel Foucault, Madness and Civilization

This part turns from artistic representation of madness to madness's clinical documentation, a turn this treatise considers essential rather than merely supplementary, because the nineteenth-century invention of psychiatric photography developed, in close historical parallel with the period's painterly Ophelia-mania (Part II), its own visual grammar for representing exactly the condition Lear's flower scene stages: the mad body's continued, often heightened, expressiveness under observation by a skeptical or clinically curious witness.

9.1  Hugh Diamond and the Birth of a Genre

Hugh Welch Diamond (1808–1886), superintendent of the Surrey County Lunatic Asylum, is credited by multiple sources as, in the words of one historical survey, the "father of psychiatric photography". The British Psychological Society's retrospective on his career frames his project's ambition and its limits together: Diamond's most consuming interest was in depicting moments of madness — the slight elevation of the eyebrows that hinted at a feeling of suspicion, the vacant look in the eye of one who saw no purpose in living, an ambition the same source situates within a much longer visual-historical tradition: since at least the 13th century, artists have been fascinated by insanity, producing hundreds of images, most stylised and stereotypic, of "madness" and "the madman" (or woman). Diamond's photographic innovation was to claim, for the first time, mechanical objectivity for this older iconographic tradition — to present the camera's output not as one more stylized artistic convention but as scientific evidence.

The claim did not survive close scholarly scrutiny, and the most useful modern reassessment of Diamond's project, published in ScienceDirect, reaches a verdict directly relevant to this treatise's governing concern with costume, performance, and "fantastical" dressing: Diamond found nothing intrinsic in these women's bodies or faces that demarcate them as mentally ill. Rather, Diamond had to rely on historically accepted iconography — clothing, props, symbolism — as opposed to data about facial expressions that he could consider to be objective and neutral. This finding is, for the present treatise's purposes, the single most important piece of evidence in the entire psychiatric-photography literature, because it confirms from within clinical history itself the same insight this treatise has been developing from within theatrical and art history: that "madness," visually, is not a natural fact awaiting documentary capture but a costume, an iconographic assemblage, that even a supposedly objective camera could only render legible by recourse to pre-existing cultural conventions of dress and prop. Diamond's sitters, in other words, were no less "fantastically dressed" by the apparatus of nineteenth-century psychiatric diagnosis than Lear is dressed by the Folio's stage direction; the costume in Diamond's case simply wore the borrowed authority of medical science rather than theatrical convention.

9.2  Charcot, the Salpêtrière, and the Performance of Symptom

Jean-Martin Charcot's later, even more theatrically explicit practice at the Salpêtrière hospital in Paris extends Diamond's project into territory this treatise's argument anticipates with particular force. The Psychiatric Times survey of madness's visual-historical representation notes that Charcot, working in collaboration with Paul Richer, took advantage of the new technology of photography to capture images of their charges, with a stated dual purpose: either to "freeze the features" of patients to allow physicians an opportunity to more effectively identify physiognomical anomalies or to freeze the behavior of "lunatics" in order to flag patterns and discrete stages in their actions. The same survey records the existence of Charcot's famous public clinical demonstrations, captured in the well-known 1887 painting A Clinical Lesson with Charcot at the Salpêtrière by André Brouillet — a painting that depicts, with theatrical staging conventions explicitly borrowed from the proscenium stage (raked audience, central illuminated performer, attendant supporting cast), a hysteric patient's symptoms being publicly displayed for a lecture-hall audience of physicians.

A scholarly examination of this material preserved in a Penn State-hosted academic paper extends the diagnosis-as-performance argument with direct reference to artistic style, recording that some nineteenth-century doctors developed diagnostic models in which a patient's mental state could be inferred from their own artistic style — childlike, primitive draughtsmanship signalling dementia, an unusual or "unreal" use of colour signalling other categories of disturbance. The principle survives intact even where the historical record of its specific clinical taxonomy is incomplete: nineteenth-century psychiatry developed, in close parallel with and partial dependence upon nineteenth-century art criticism's own vocabulary, an entire grammar for reading deviation from naturalistic representational convention as a direct symptom of underlying mental pathology. This is the clinical mirror-image of the surrealist embrace (Part IV) of exactly the same representational deviations as liberatory rather than pathological; Dalí's "melting, almost fleshy flower forms" and the asylum patient's "unreal use of colour" are, formally, describable in nearly identical terms — the difference lies entirely in whether the deviation is being celebrated as imaginative freedom or catalogued as diagnostic evidence.

9.3  Rawling's Iconography and the Pose That Persists

Katherine Rawling's scholarly study of late-Victorian asylum photography, cited across several of the sources consulted for this treatise, supplies the specific historiographical term this entire section has been circling: images of patients' bodies and faces were used to convey particular diagnostic conclusions, but — and this is the crucial qualifying finding Rawling's work establishes — the photographs were, per the Staffordshire Asylums historical blog's summary, not always neutral and objective; rather, ideas of the nature of madness rather than neutral photographic reproduction often crept into the images, some of which were posed or manipulated, with the unfortunate documented consequence that photographs from within the asylum walls started to be used to illustrate medical textbooks, but they often tended to skew the representation of a particular illness into an exaggerated archetype.

The Cambridge Core retrospective on Diamond's specific legacy adds a further, ethically complicating dimension that resists any simple verdict of pure exploitation, observing that his photographs demonstrate that there is nothing intrinsically found in these women's bodies or faces that demarcates them as mentally ill while simultaneously noting a contrasting double effect: the same images immortalise[] and give[] power [to the subjects] while also representing the face of mental illness in the 19th century, an inherently devalued group, a doubling the same source attributes partly to the subjects' own visible agency within the frame — the women either stare at the camera (and directly at the viewer) or avert their gaze, refusing to engage and be objectified. This is, this treatise notes, almost exactly the dynamic Edgar's "side-piercing sight" registers in Lear's own scene: a witnessing structure in which the mad figure's continued capacity for direct address, for self-assertion ("I am the king himself"), persistently destabilizes the observer's attempt to settle into a position of pure clinical or aesthetic distance.

9.4  Foucault's Renaissance: Madness as Visible Truth

Michel Foucault's 1961 Histoire de la folie (translated into English in abridged form as Madness and Civilization) provides the theoretical apparatus that allows this treatise to connect the asylum-photography material directly back to its Shakespearean point of origin, because Foucault names King Lear explicitly, repeatedly, as a primary historical exhibit in his account of how Western culture's relationship to madness changed across the early modern period. Scholar Stuart Elden's close textual study of Foucault's engagement with Shakespeare preserves Foucault's key formulation concerning the historical moment Lear's play occupies: madness was denied the imaginary liberty that still allowed it to flourish at the time of the Renaissance. Not so long ago it was still visible in the light of day, as in King Lear or Don Quixote, but within the space of half a century it found itself a recluse — confined, that is, by the mid-seventeenth-century institutional development Foucault elsewhere calls the Great Confinement.

Foucault's broader argument, as SparkNotes' summary of the relevant chapter distills it, holds that for Shakespeare and Cervantes, madness was beyond appeal; it is situated in ultimate regions — madness, in this Renaissance frame, functions as a kind of ultimate limit, the worst thing that can happen to a human being precisely because it destroys humanity, and yet this destruction is staged, in Lear's case, as paradoxically productive of the play's most truthful speech. The GradeSaver study guide's summary of Foucault's preface captures the resulting paradox in terms this treatise's title-image makes vivid: the madman plays an important role in tales and fables… he starts to play a central role in narratives, where the "fool" is often the "guardian of truth", a paradox Foucault names directly: the madman is both outside and fundamental to human knowledge.

The Then & Now historical-philosophy blog's summary of Foucault's account adds one further crucial dimension this treatise has been implicitly tracking through its discussion of Lear's "natural fool of fortune" self-identification: Foucault's analysis of the period's broader logic of "animality," in which unchained animality could be mastered only by disciplining and brutalizing, and in which madness threatened modern man only with that return to the bleak world of beast and things, to their unfettered freedom. Lear's flower-crowning, on this Foucauldian reading, performs exactly this threatened "return… to the bleak world of beast and things" — a king reduced not merely to ordinary humanity but past it, into a category of being defined by unfettered, undiscriminating contact with the vegetal and animal world the crown was originally instituted to rule over and discriminate from.

Complexity Coda · IX

Foucault's clinical gaze sorts, categorises, and diagnoses from a position of presumed objectivity outside the phenomenon it observes — precisely the stance most change-management methodology asks its consultants and sponsoring executives to occupy: diagnose the organisation's dysfunction, prescribe the appropriate intervention, monitor compliance with the treatment plan. Stacey and Mowles's complex responsive processes tradition mounts, in effect, a Foucauldian objection to this entire posture: there is no clinical position outside the organisational system from which an observer could diagnose it without already being one of the local interactions constituting it. The consultant administering the change diagnosis is never standing where Diamond's camera or Charcot's lecture-hall audience stood, however much the engagement letter implies otherwise; they are already inside the field of relationships their diagnosis claims to describe from outside, and the costume of clinical objectivity this part traced onto Lear's asylum photography is, on this reading, as available to the modern change practitioner as it ever was to the nineteenth-century alienist.

There is no programmer · the gaze from nowhere

The clinical gaze claims the one position complexity science says no participant can occupy: outside the system, parameters in hand, the programmer who sets the conditions and is not himself set by them. Patricia Shaw's most radical move is to deny this position to everyone — consultant, physician, change leader, king. In the Santa Fe simulations there was a programmer holding the edge-of-chaos conditions steady from outside; “in human interaction,” Shaw writes, “we can have no analogy for the programmer.” Diamond's camera and Charcot's lecture-hall stage the fantasy of the external observer; the change consultant's diagnostic framework restages it in a boardroom. But there is no outside. The alienist is in the ward; the consultant is in the figuration; Lear discovers on the heath that there was never a dry vantage from which to watch the storm — only the storm, and the question of who you turn out to be inside it.

Everyone who claims to stand outside the weather is already wet.

Stacey's account of organisations as figurations constituted by power relations rather than as systems available to neutral external diagnosis draws directly on Elias's insistence that power is "polymorphous" — many-sided, inherent in every relationship, never the simple possession of one party over another. The Foucauldian clinical gaze this part traces through asylum photography assumes the opposite: a diagnostician positioned outside the power relations of the ward, able to observe and categorise from a vantage the patient's own position cannot occupy. Elias's polymorphous power, like Stacey's complex responsive processes, denies any participant — consultant, executive, facilitator — this fully external vantage; the change practitioner administering a diagnosis is one more node in the figuration's fluctuating power-balance, not a camera mounted safely outside it, and the costume of clinical objectivity this part traced onto Lear's asylum photography survives, on this account, only by suppressing its own position within the very power relations it claims merely to observe.

X. The Panel and the Gutter: Graphic Novel Adaptations and the Grammar of Closure

The graphic novel medium's engagement with Lear's flower-crowning offers this treatise its clearest available case study in how a single textual moment can be rendered according to three almost entirely opposed visual logics within the same broad medium, because three significant English-language graphic adaptations of the play — by Gareth Hinds, Ian Pollock, and Richard Appignanesi — exist in sufficient critical proximity to one another that comparative analysis becomes possible in a way it rarely is across the more dispersedly produced traditions of painting or film.

10.1  McCloud's Closure as Critical Apparatus

A substantial comparative essay on these three adaptations, published on the platform Medium and explicitly organized around Scott McCloud's comics theory, supplies this part's governing analytical vocabulary. The essay's author explains the foundational concept directly: McCloud defines closure as the "phenomenon of observing the parts but perceiving the whole", a cognitive operation comics uniquely demand of readers because, unlike continuous theatrical or cinematic performance, the graphic novel is made up of panels, individual images separated by gutters the reader's imagination must actively bridge. This apparatus is directly relevant to the flower-crowning scene because that scene, in its original theatrical form, unfolds continuously — Lear walks on, speaks, is recognized, continues speaking, exits — whereas any comics adaptation must necessarily fragment this continuous unfolding into discrete panels, forcing the artist to choose which instants of the scene's continuous duration deserve full pictorial elaboration and which can be left to the reader's inferential closure across the gutter.

Ensemble improvisation · accepting the offer

McCloud's gutter — the blank between panels where the reader manufactures the motion neither panel contains — is the comics form's confession that meaning is made between the parties, never delivered whole. This is exactly Keith Johnstone's first law of improvisation, the one Shaw and Stacey place at the heart of the complexity approach to change: accept the offer. The panel makes an offer; the reader must accept it across the gutter, allowing themselves to be changed by it, or the sequence dies on the page as surely as an improviser kills a scene by blocking. A change communication is a panel; the corridor conversation that completes or refuses it is the gutter; and the organisation that pours all its effort into the panels and none into the gutters has misunderstood where the reading actually happens. You cannot draw the gutter — you can only make an offer good enough that someone crosses it.

Meaning lives in the gutter, not the panel; in the offer accepted, not the offer made.

10.2  Hinds's Faithful Eclecticism

Gareth Hinds's 2008 adaptation, the best-documented of the three thanks to the artist's own promotional materials, pursues a strategy of careful tonal modulation through color and line rather than radical stylistic distortion. Hinds's own description of his method records a deliberately eclectic period-blending approach: the setting is a melange of historical periods and styles, executed with a range of styles and materials, but the majority of the work was done with technical pen and watercolor. The Medium essay's close analysis of Hinds's coloring choices across the play's emotional arc demonstrates that this apparent eclecticism is in fact systematically deployed for psychological effect: comparing the love-test scene's palette to the storm scene's, the essay observes that during the love test, each entity on the page is made up of a blend of soft yellows, reds, blues, etc.… the shapes of the characters remain neat and defined, marking a time where Lear still has a joyful, ignorant view of the world, whereas in the storm and subsequent madness sequences Lear's color and shape… are visually dark and panicked. Reader reception, captured in Goodreads reviews of the volume, registers the resulting visual intensity as effective if demanding: one reviewer praises specifically the sgraffito storm scene as particularly effective, while noting that Hinds wisely… keeps large parts of the original dialog so the poetry and the passion of the play are undiluted, deploying watercolor washed illustrations that contrast with adjacent sharply inked lines to highlight the King at the moments of greatest emotional extremity.

10.3  Pollock's Grotesque, Fully Embraced

Ian Pollock's adaptation pursues the opposite strategy from Hinds's careful tonal modulation, and the Medium essay's characterization of his approach reads almost as a direct visual translation of Kott's theoretical vocabulary from Part VIII into the comics medium: Pollock's eccentric, surreal drawing style may be confusing and cartoonish, but it reveals his personal perspective of the play as, not a tragedy, but a production of the grotesque. The essay's analysis of why this stylistic choice succeeds, rather than simply trivializing the source material, turns on exactly the freedom-through-distortion logic this treatise has tracked since the surrealist material in Part IV: the real-world impossibility of his depictions gives him the freedom to provide scenes that would be otherwise jarring and absurd in other mediums. Where Hinds aims for a controlled, legible emotional itinerary across the play's arc, Pollock's explicit visual grotesquerie — described elsewhere in the same comparative essay as including, in a separate adaptation by a third artist, Hillyer's animalistic Goneril and Regan — gives the comics medium permission to render Lear's flower-crowned madness with the same uninhibited categorical violation surrealist painting permits itself, unconstrained by the reputational caution that more "respectable" prestige adaptations (Hinds's, aimed explicitly at a young-adult educational market) might otherwise impose.

10.4  The Medium's Own Argument for Itself

The comparative essay's concluding judgment deserves quotation in full because it articulates, more directly than this treatise's other sources manage to for their respective media, an explicit theory of why comics specifically might be peculiarly well-suited to rendering Shakespearean madness: the true strength of the comic is how personal it is. From its grammar and structure of panels and gutters to its need to interpose text and image, the graphic novel lives on subjectivity. It requires adaptation to create and interpretation to read. This is, this treatise notes, a more explicit and more medium-conscious version of the claim Foucault's account of asylum photography (Part IX) makes implicitly: that no representational technology, however apparently mechanical or objective its apparatus (the photographic plate; the comics panel grid), escapes the necessity of interpretive, subjective mediation when its subject is madness. The comics medium's structural requirement that readers actively perform closure across the gutter — bridging, with their own imagination, what the artist has chosen not to draw — makes this mediation visible and acknowledged in a way film and photography's illusion of continuous, unmediated capture tend to disguise.

10.5  Pedagogical Afterlife

A final note on reception is warranted, because it bears on this treatise's broader argument about the image's cultural durability across very different audience constituencies. Booklist's selection of Hinds's volume as one of the 10 best graphic novels of 2008 for youth, and the volume's subsequent adoption — confirmed by classroom-resource sites such as A Book and a Hug, which describes the book as making Shakespeare approachable for younger readers while using Shakespeare's original language — establishes that the flower-crowned Lear's most recent significant vehicle of cultural transmission to new generations of readers is not the opera house, the art museum, or the repertory cinema this treatise's other parts have surveyed, but the middle-school and high-school classroom, mediated through exactly the visual-grotesque and visual-eclectic strategies examined above. The image's journey from Jacobean stage direction to compulsory educational curriculum, by way of Millais's Royal Academy, Dalí's Pop-era print suites, Kozintsev's Cold War steppe, and Reimann's standing chord, is itself a remarkable cultural itinerary, and this treatise's next two parts will extend that itinerary into fashion's runways and, finally, into the prompt-strings of contemporary generative image systems.

Complexity Coda · X

McCloud's account of comics "closure" — meaning constructed by the reader in the gutter between panels, not supplied wholesale by either panel alone — supplies an exact image for how meaning is actually made during organisational change, on Stacey's relational account of the process. No change communication, however carefully drafted, transmits its intended meaning directly into employees' heads the way a single panel might seem to transmit its content directly into a reader's eye; meaning is constructed, gutter by gutter, in the gaps between the official announcement and what people actually say to one another about it afterwards, in corridors and instant messages the steering committee never sees. A change programme that attends only to the panels — the town-hall slide deck, the FAQ document, the leadership video — and never to the gutters between them is managing only half of what McCloud's theory shows is actually doing the representational work.

Mead's gesture-response loop, requiring a respondent to complete any gesture's meaning, supplies the theoretical name for what McCloud calls closure: the reader's response in the gutter is the missing half of the comics medium's own gesture, without which the panel's meaning remains incomplete. Stacey's account of organisational sense-making treats every change communication the identical way — incomplete until the responses it provokes, in corridors and private messages the official channel cannot see, have completed its meaning. The gutter is not a gap to be minimised through ever more detailed panels (ever more exhaustive slide decks, ever more granular FAQ documents) but the actual site where an organisation's meaning-making happens, structurally inaccessible to direct authorial control by the party who drew the panels on either side of it.

XI. Sarabande: Alexander McQueen and the Couture of Mortal Sovereignty

Ripeness is all.— King Lear, V.ii — Edgar, two words against the whole machinery of control

Of every cultural register this treatise surveys, fashion might seem the least likely to sustain serious comparison with Lear's flower-crowned scene — runway spectacle and Jacobean tragedy occupy, on the surface, almost opposite positions on any imaginable scale of cultural seriousness. This part argues the opposite: that Alexander McQueen's body of work, and specifically his Spring/Summer 2007 collection Sarabande, constitutes one of the most theoretically precise restatements of this treatise's entire argument to be found anywhere in its survey, articulated not through textual allusion (there is no evidence McQueen was thinking of Shakespeare specifically) but through an independently arrived-at understanding of flowers as the visible sign of mortality worn, deliberately, on a living and sovereign body.

11.1  The Finale Dress and Its Stated Philosophy

The Sarabande collection's closing look, extensively documented across fashion journalism, consisted, per Wikipedia's detailed account, of a dress covered with silk and fresh flowers, which, having been hastily sewn on, began falling off on the runway, creating what critics regarded as a moment of serendipitous beauty. McQueen's own explanation of the design's underlying logic, preserved by AnOther magazine, is the single most directly relevant primary-source statement this entire treatise has located outside the Shakespearean text itself: I used flowers because they die. … Nothing is forever. There was a fragility to this collection, in the idea that nothing is permanent, that beauty disappears.

Open systems · why the dying flower tells the truth

McQueen chose fresh flowers — flowers that die — over silk ones that last, and the choice carries a complexity-science argument McQueen states more sharply than the textbooks. A living system, McMillan insists, is defined precisely by its impermanence: it is "open to its environment in order to exchange energy and matter," it survives only by continuously dissipating and renewing itself, and the moment it closes itself off and tries to last unchanged, it is no longer alive but merely preserved. The silk flower is the closed system — permanent, sealed, beautiful, dead. The fresh flower is the open system — exchanging, decaying, alive precisely because it will not last. McQueen's "nothing is forever" is therefore not a melancholy flourish but a statement about what life is: that mortality is not the enemy of the living thing but its definition, the price and proof of being open rather than embalmed. And the falling flowers complete the argument by accident — sewn on hastily, detaching mid-walk, the dress enacting its own dissipation in real time before the audience. The organisation that craves permanence, that wants its change to set and hold and last, is asking to become the silk flower: flawless, finished, and no longer alive. Only what can die is alive; only what stays open keeps exchanging; and the flower that falls tells a truth the flower that lasts cannot.

The silk flower lasts forever because it was never alive; the fresh flower dies because it is — and McQueen sewed on the ones that fall.

This statement requires no elaborate interpretive bridge to connect to Lear's own scene; it states, in McQueen's own contemporary fashion-designer's vocabulary, exactly the logic this treatise's Part III identified in Frazer's corn-king material — flowers as the visible sign of a sovereignty (here, the sovereignty of "beauty" rather than political kingship, but structurally identical) that is constitutively temporary, that wears its own coming death as its most visible ornament. Wikipedia's account of the collection's broader curatorial reception confirms this reading was immediately legible to contemporary critics: although the flowers falling off the final dress was a serendipitous effect, many critics have taken it as a demonstration of the fragility of life, with the curator Claire Wilcox's later retrospective judgment recorded as describing the collection's governing aesthetic as one in which function and beauty were disdained for a luxurious impracticality and historicist beauty.

11.2  Decaying Grandeur as Explicit Collection Theme

The collection's full conceptual frame, as documented by Wikipedia, was an exploration of fragility and decaying grandeur expressed through floral motifs, drawing inspiration in part from Stanley Kubrick's 1975 film Barry Lyndon, a film, Wikipedia notes, known for its themes of fatalism and romanticism. The phrase "decaying grandeur" deserves to be set in direct apposition with this treatise's recurring vocabulary for Lear's own condition — a deposed king, still dressed (however "fantastically") in the visible residue of grandeur, his authority decayed but not yet entirely extinguished, his speech still carrying the cadence and the imperatives of command even as his body has been stripped of every external marker of rank except the self-assembled, vegetal one. The Guardian's contemporary review, as relayed by Wikipedia, registered some ambivalence about the literal wearability of this vision — the dresses, the review noted, seemed "more like costume" than wearable fashion — but this very ambivalence, this treatise suggests, is structurally identical to the ambivalence built into the Folio's own stage direction: "fantastically dressed" already names, four centuries earlier, exactly the costume/clothing distinction the Guardian critic independently rediscovers, the sense that what is being worn exceeds ordinary garment function and enters the register of theatrical or ritual costume.

11.3  A Career-Long Preoccupation

Sarabande was not an isolated gesture within McQueen's larger body of work but the most explicit statement of a recurring concern. Vogue Hong Kong's account of a much later, posthumous Sarah Burton collection for the house traces the lineage explicitly: from the spring/summer 07 Sarabande show, with its gowns constructed using fresh flowers that cascaded down the designs, to the 'Rose dress' of autumn/winter 2019 and the laser-cut blooms that traversed the runway in spring/summer 2020, over the years flowers have been something of a touchstone for Alexander McQueen. Burton's own statement accompanying a 2021 collection inspired by anemones extends McQueen's mortality-logic into a register of explicit bodily identification: the women wearing the anemone dresses almost become like flowers… but amplified, grounded, radiant and strong — a formulation that inverts the direction of metamorphosis this treatise has tracked through the surrealist material in Part IV (where human figures dissolve into vegetal form as a register of vulnerability or loss of coherent selfhood) into a register of empowerment, the flower-identification figured as amplification rather than dissolution of the wearer's strength.

This inversion is worth pausing over because it identifies a genuine point of historical and conceptual development this treatise should not elide: where Lear's flower-crowning, Ophelia's flower-distribution, and the surrealist vegetal body all code botanical metamorphosis as a register of loss, dissolution, or at minimum profound ambivalence, McQueen's house, by the time of Burton's tenure, had begun to code the identical floral-bodily fusion as a register of strength and survival. The retrospective exhibition Roses, documented by AnOther magazine, makes this evolving philosophy explicit, describing its own thematic frame as an exploration of how the symbolism of flowers is rooted in the power of nature, with Burton's curatorial narration, per the same source, detailing the memories behind the collections, from trips to country gardens to reminiscences on embroideries — a far gentler, more consoling relationship to botanical imagery than anything Lear's cornfield, Cordelia's catalogue of poisonous weeds, or Dalí's "violent" Les Fruits suite permits.

11.4  The Catwalk's Longer Floral History

A broader survey of floral fashion history, published by the florist-affiliated Ellermann journal, situates McQueen's specific contribution within a much longer twentieth-century arc this treatise's argument benefits from seeing in full. The same source traces a lineage from Christian Dior's 1947 New Look — in which Dior frequently described women as flowers themselves, with dresses… named after tulips, lilies, and roses — through the self-consciously "intellectual" floral interrogation the source attributes to Comme des Garçons and Yohji Yamamoto in the 1980s, whose collections stripped florals of sweetness, presenting instead blackened blossoms, asymmetrical petals, distressed textures, and garments that looked partially decayed — a direct anticipation, a full two decades before Sarabande, of exactly the decay-aestheticization McQueen would later make his own signature. The same survey's account of Gucci's Alessandro Michele era describes a still further mutation, in which Michele layered Renaissance florals, antique upholstery motifs, embroidered snakes, and overgrown garden imagery into dense visual narratives, such that florals became eccentric rather than conventionally feminine — a maximalist, hybridizing aesthetic this treatise notes bears a strong family resemblance to the "composite plant creatures" Dalí's late floral suites (Part IV) produced through an entirely separate, decades-earlier print-medium process.

What this longer survey establishes, for this treatise's purposes, is that fashion's relationship to botanical imagery has cycled repeatedly, across the twentieth and twenty-first centuries, through exactly the same oscillation this treatise has tracked across painting, theatre, opera, and dance: flowers as consoling beauty (Dior, the Burton-era anemone dresses) versus flowers as decay, menace, and category violation (Kawakubo, Yamamoto, McQueen's Sarabande). That a single runway house's own internal aesthetic history — McQueen's mortality-coded flowers giving way, under his successor, to Burton's strength-coded flowers — can recapitulate this entire four-century oscillation within a span of fewer than fifteen years is, this treatise suggests, the strongest available evidence that the oscillation itself, rather than either of its poles taken alone, constitutes the genuinely durable cultural structure this whole document has been attempting to describe.

Complexity Coda · XI

McQueen's stated design philosophy — flowers chosen because they die, because nothing is forever — and the unplanned mechanical failure of Sarabande's sewn-on blooms detaching mid-walk supply this treatise's clearest fashion-world instance of decoronation occurring by accident rather than design, the collection's most powerful moment arising from exactly the element no one had scripted. Most change programmes are designed to eliminate this kind of accident, treating any deviation from the planned choreography as a failure of execution to be corrected before the next show. Stacey's edge-of-chaos literature, and the facilitation practice gathered in Kevin Flinn's A Web of Relations, both suggest the opposite emphasis: that the unplanned departure from the choreography — the moment the flowers actually fall, rather than the moment they were sewn on to look as though they might — is frequently where an organisation's most truthful and most memorable change actually becomes visible, and that a facilitator's skill lies less in preventing such moments than in knowing how to work with what they reveal once they have happened.

Streatfield's paradox of control supplies the right frame for McQueen's own retrospective account of the falling flowers: a designer who had planned meticulously for the collection's central image nonetheless found that image's most resonant version supplied by mechanical failure rather than by design, and who had, crucially, retained enough presence and skill within the unplanned moment to let it become the show's defining image rather than treating it as an error to be edited out of the record. This is Stacey's "courage to go on participating creatively despite not knowing" rendered as fashion-week choreography: not the abandonment of design intention, but design intention held loosely enough to recognise, in real time, when the figuration's own unplanned behaviour has produced something more truthful than the plan it momentarily failed to execute.

Complexity Coda · XI

McQueen built catwalks that staged collapse as couture — garments coming apart, models crowned in horns and weeds, the runway as a controlled space invaded by the wild. His genius was to understand fashion as a complex system poised at the edge: too much order and it is dead commerce, too little and it is chaos, and the living work happens in the narrow band between, where the designer sets a few enabling constraints and lets the collection self-organise toward something no sketch predicted. The flower-crown is the oldest McQueen gesture — the sovereign body re-dressed in the matter of the field — and it carries the same change-management truth his shows enacted: that the most powerful order is the one that looks, to the frightened eye of the boardroom, like the beginning of breakdown.

❧
Movement Four
Two Civilizations, and the Generative Return
The Daoist wilds and a second civilization’s flower-crowned exile set beside the machine-generated prompt and the first sketch of a general theory — East and West, ancient and synthetic, circling one image.

XII. 狂 Kuáng and the Wilds: A Daoist Counter-Reading of Sovereign Madness

The great earth-clod blows out breath; its name is wind. So long as it does not blow, nothing happens. But when it blows, then the ten thousand hollows begin to cry wildly… And when the fierce gusts have passed on, then all the hollows are empty again.— Zhuangzi, Inner Chapters (trans. Watson)

This treatise's penultimate substantive part turns to a philosophical tradition entirely outside the European lineage that has organized every preceding section, in order to test whether the structural logic this document has been tracking — sovereignty undone by, and re-clothed in, nature's own disordered abundance — names something culturally specific to the post-Renaissance West, or whether it touches a more broadly available register of human thought about the relationship between reason, rule, and the "wild." Classical Chinese philosophy, and the Zhuangzian strand of early Daoism specifically, offers a sustained, positively-valenced theory of exactly the condition Lear's flower-crowning otherwise stages as unambiguous catastrophe — a theory worth setting beside the Western tradition not to dissolve the differences between them but to sharpen, by contrast, what is most historically and culturally particular about Shakespeare's own treatment.

12.1  The Zhuangzian Wilds

The scholarly study most directly relevant to this comparison is identified in its own chapter title with unusual directness: "The Wilds, Untamed, and Spontaneity: Zhuangist Views of Madness," a chapter within a longer monograph on madness and self-cultivation in early Chinese philosophy and medicine. The chapter's own abstract states its governing claim plainly: it offers a "positive" account of madness, exploring the way we can understand an inherent value in madness within the Zhuangzian textual tradition. This is, on its face, a radically different starting orientation from anything available within the European lineage this treatise has surveyed — even Kott's grotesque (Part VIII), which strips Lear's madness of recoverable tragic dignity, never proposes that the madness itself carries positive philosophical value; it remains, for Kott as for Shakespeare, a catastrophe, however illuminating its incidental insights.

The Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy's account of the Zhuangzi text's broader reception history situates this positive valuation of unconventional, "wild" states of mind within a specific socio-political function the text came to serve across Chinese intellectual history: the great popularity of the Zhuangzi among Chinese literati who saw within it support for a withdrawal from a life of social and political service into a private life of reclusion and self-cultivation. Crucially, the same source establishes the explicit philosophical opposition this withdrawal was understood to enact: if Confucianism came to stand for the foundational philosophy of this ethos of self-sacrifice [in service to the state], the Daoism of the Zhuangzi came to stand for its opposite, the escape from societal pressure into an individual path of freedom.

The rainforest in the clay-pit · the garden as 無為 made real

Elizabeth McMillan supplies a living instance of what Daoist non-forcing cultivation looks like when it is actually practised as management, and it is — precisely — a garden grown from ruined ground. Her chief exemplar of complexity-led leadership is Tim Smit, who "discovered and restored the Lost Gardens of Heligan" and then built "the ecological masterpiece of the Eden Project" — vast translucent domes housing "the only rainforest in captivity," raised, McMillan stresses, "in a disused china clay pit in Cornwall." A dead industrial wound — the very image of exhausted, infertile, used-up ground — made into the most luxuriant garden in Britain. And Smit's method "owes nothing to conventional management techniques"; his philosophy is "based not on notions of control but on openness and a belief in the ability of individuals." This is the exact inverse of Lear, and the inversion is total. Lear begins on fertile, ordered ground (a whole kingdom, intact) and, by an act of control — the engineered love-test — reduces it to the blasted heath, the barren field of weeds. Smit begins on the blasted ground — the dead clay-pit, Lear's heath made literal — and, by the refusal of control, by openness and the foster-nursing of conditions, grows a rainforest. Lear is the gardener who, insisting on control, turns a garden into a wasteland; Smit is the gardener who, releasing control, turns a wasteland into a garden. The weed-crown and the rainforest-in-the-clay-pit are the two possible harvests of the same ground, and the only variable is whether the sovereign forces or fosters. Lear controlled a kingdom into a heath; Smit fostered a clay-pit into a rainforest — the same barren ground yields a weed-crown or a paradise, and the only difference is whether the gardener grips or lets grow.

Control turns the kingdom into the heath; 無為 turns the dead clay-pit into a rainforest — the ground is the same, and only the gardener's grip decides which harvest it bears.

12.2  Sovereignty Refused versus Sovereignty Stripped

This opposition allows this treatise to name, with unusual precision, the structural difference separating the Zhuangzian wild sage from Shakespeare's flower-crowned Lear, and the difference is one of agency and timing rather than of surface imagery. The Zhuangzian withdrawal into "wildness" is, crucially, voluntary and anticipatory: the literatus chooses reclusion, chooses to step outside the structures of office and ritual propriety, in advance of any external catastrophe forcing the choice upon him. Lear's flower-crowned condition is the precise opposite: it is involuntary, reactive, forced upon him by his daughters' betrayal and the storm's exposure, arriving only after his sovereignty has already been stripped by external forces entirely outside his control. Where the Zhuangzian sage's "madness" (insofar as the term even applies to the deliberately cultivated unconventionality the tradition describes) is a philosophical achievement, an enviable freedom purchased by clear-eyed renunciation, Lear's madness is an affliction, a collapse, something the play never permits us to read as anything he would have chosen.

And yet — this is the point at which the comparison becomes genuinely productive rather than merely contrastive — both traditions converge on identifying "wildness," however differently arrived at, as the condition under which a certain register of truth becomes speakable that was not speakable from within the structures of ordinary social and political propriety. The Confucian official, bound by ritual hierarchy (禮, lǐ), cannot easily say to his sovereign what the Zhuangzian recluse, freed from office, can say with impunity; precisely so, Lear's "fool of fortune," freed (however catastrophically) from the obligations of kingship's own ritual propriety, can say to Gloucester things — "a dog's obeyed in office," "robes and furred gowns hide all" — that King Lear, fully sovereign and fully sane, never once says across the entire first three acts of the play, when he is busy enacting exactly the kind of ritualized, performance-demanding authority (the love-test) the Zhuangzian tradition holds in such explicit suspicion.

12.3  道理勢: The Way, the Pattern, and the Configurational Force

This convergence bears directly on a triad that organizes this treatise's reading throughout — the recurring categories of 道 (dào, the way), 理 (lǐ, principle or pattern), and 勢 (shì, configurational force or strategic potential).
