By the mid-1830s, however, declining sales, poverty, and a deteriorating mental state led to his admission, first to a private asylum at High Beech in Epping Forest in 1837, and then, following a remarkable escape and eighty-mile walk home across four days in July 1841 — during which Clare, by his own subsequent prose account addressed to an imaginary wife, ate grass by the roadside to stave off hunger — to permanent committal at the Northampton General Lunatic Asylum at the close of 1841, where he remained for the rest of his life and continued, with what one biographer calls "strangely unquenched lyric impulse," to write some of his finest poetry until his death in 1864.

25.2  "I'm John Clare Now... I Was Shakespeare Formerly"

What makes Clare's case directly relevant to this treatise's argument, rather than simply a poignant biographical parallel, is the specific and repeatedly documented content of his delusion during the asylum years: Clare did not merely admire Shakespeare, he claimed, with calm and apparently sincere conviction, to be him. Visitors recorded the poet's flat assertion — "I'm John Clare now... I was Byron and Shakespeare formerly... sometimes they called me Shakespeare, sometimes Byron, sometimes Clare" — and asylum staff documented his habit of quoting extensively from Shakespeare's and Byron's verse while presenting the borrowed lines as his own original composition, not as plagiarism but as a sincerely confused claim of authorial identity, the boundary between self and source author having genuinely dissolved for him in a way his doctors' own clinical notes treated as a defining symptom of his condition. Clare also produced, during his asylum years, an extended original poem titled Child Harold, explicitly modelled on and substantially appropriating Byron's Childe Harold's Pilgrimage, written from within the same identificatory delusion.

This treatise wants to register, with appropriate care, exactly what this identification means for the argument sustained across its survey. Clare did not, so far as the documentary record establishes, specifically claim to have written King Lear, or specifically re-enact the flower-crown scene in any performed or documented way; the identification with Shakespeare seems to have operated at the level of generalized authorial identity rather than any specific re-staging of any specific play. And yet the structural resonance is difficult to set aside: a poet whose entire prior career had been built on a uniquely intimate, almost dissolving identification with the natural world — a poet who famously claimed "I found my poems in the fields and only wrote them down," refusing even the conventional Romantic distinction between the poet's shaping imagination and nature's own inherent expressiveness — eats grass on a roadside during a flight from institutional confinement, then goes on, within the asylum itself, to claim the literal identity of the playwright who two centuries earlier had staged a king crowned in the field's own indifferent vegetation as the ultimate emblem of reason's collapse.

25.3  Edward Bond's Return to Clare

It is, for this treatise's purposes, an unusually pleasing closure of a circle this treatise has already traced once, in its discussion of Edward Bond's 1971 Lear, that Bond himself returned four years later, in his 1975 play The Fool, directly to the historical figure this part has just surveyed: the labouring-class poet whose documented identification with nature gradually curdled, under the pressures of poverty and institutional confinement, into the genuine, sustained delusion of having become Shakespeare. That Bond followed his own aggressive political rewriting of Lear with a play directly about the historical poet whose actual lived madness involved claiming Shakespeare's identity suggests that Bond himself recognized the same structural kinship this treatise has been arguing for throughout its survey: that Clare's documented asylum delusion and Lear's own flower-crowned "I am the king himself" are two instances of an identical underlying gesture — an authority claim sustained, with full sincerity, after every external, material, and social ground for that claim has been stripped away.

25.4  Living on the Wrong Side of Foucault's Threshold

This part closes by naming what Clare's lived case contributes that no purely performative or visual-art instance, however powerful, could supply on its own: direct access to lived, rather than represented, correspondence with the motif's underlying structure. Clare's twenty-three years at Northampton, compared with Lear's brief, theatrically compressed restoration to Cordelia, registers as this treatise's starkest available reminder that the asylum regime this treatise's earlier parts examined through Foucault and through clinical photography had, by Clare's own lifetime, already closed off the more circulatory, more dignified medieval and Renaissance relation to madness that Foucault locates in Shakespeare's own historical moment. Clare lived, in other words, on the wrong side of exactly the historical threshold this treatise's account of Foucault identified, and his poetry's continuing, undiminished power testifies to art's capacity to preserve a measure of the older dignity even when the institutions surrounding the poet's actual body had already, by the 1840s, withdrawn it absolutely.

Complexity Coda · XXIV

John Clare's documented identification with Shakespeare and Byron — believing himself, for a period, literally to be the playwright reincarnated — supplies this treatise's most extreme instance of the witnessed figure's own self-conception diverging from the outside world's diagnostic verdict. Change management's own version of this divergence appears whenever an organisation's self-narrative — "we are an agile, innovative culture" — persists well past the point at which outside observers, and frequently the organisation's own front-line staff, can substantiate it. Stacey's distinction between an organisation's official, espoused account of itself and the actual pattern of its local interactions is, in effect, a more clinical restatement of the gap Clare's biography stages so starkly between self-conception and outside verdict: the gap is not necessarily evidence of bad faith on anyone's part, any more than Clare's identification was simple delusion rather than a profound, if displaced, form of poetic recognition, but it is a gap a change practitioner ignores at their peril, since the organisation's actual behaviour during change will track the unexamined self-narrative far more reliably than it will track the espoused one.

Stacey's account of organisations as constituted by their espoused narrative on one hand and their actual pattern of local interaction on the other gives Clare's biography an unexpectedly direct organisational application. Clare's identification with Shakespeare was not simple delusion but a profound, displaced form of poetic recognition — his actual creative practice tracking his Shakespearean self-conception with real fidelity even where his factual biography could not. An organisation's espoused self-narrative — "we are an agile, innovative culture" — deserves something of the same complicated charity and the same clinical honesty Clare's case demands: not dismissed outright as corporate fiction, since espoused narratives shape behaviour even when factually unsupported, but never mistaken, as Lear's own court mistakes its own self-image, for an accurate report of the figuration's actual current pattern of local interaction.

The poet who thought he was Shakespeare and the king who thought he was nothing met in the same field; madness and authorship are two names for the self the institution could not hold.

XXV. 勢 Shì and the Weiqi Board: A Second Reading of Act One's Catastrophe

A skilled player does not seize territory; he builds shi — a configuration of potential so favourable that the territory falls to him without a fight.— after the strategic literature on 勢 and the game of weiqi

Part XII drew on the Zhuangzian wilds to test whether this treatise's governing structure belongs specifically to the post-Renaissance West or touches a more broadly available register of thought. This part extends that comparative gesture in a different direction, drawing on the classical Chinese military-strategic concept of 勢 (shì) and its embodiment in the board game weiqi — known in the West by its Japanese name, Go — to supply an analytical vocabulary for the mechanics of Lear's opening political collapse that this treatise's iconographic and performative survey has not, until now, directly addressed.

26.1  Shi: The Untranslatable Configuration

Sun Tzu's Art of War devotes an entire chapter to the concept of shi, and the considerable modern scholarly and military literature attempting to render the term into English has repeatedly concluded that no single Western equivalent exists. Analysts have variously glossed shi as "the alignment of forces," "the propensity of things," or — in one frequently cited official formulation — "the potential born of disposition... that only a skilled strategist can exploit to ensure victory over a superior force." Sun Tzu's own illustrative imagery for the concept reaches for natural rather than mechanical metaphors with a precision directly relevant to this treatise's broader argument about Lear's own vegetal and elemental imagery: shi, in the classical text, is what allows the velocity of cascading water to send boulders tumbling, and what allows a striking bird of prey to smash its victim to pieces purely through correctly timed positional advantage rather than through raw force. Shi names not strength itself but the configuration of a situation that converts modest force into devastating effect.

26.2  Encirclement Against Confrontation

The scholarly comparison most frequently drawn in the literature on shi contrasts weiqi's underlying strategic logic explicitly against Western chess, and the contrast proves unexpectedly precise as a lens for King Lear's own opening catastrophe. Where chess is fundamentally a confrontational, force-on-force, zero-sum game oriented toward direct capture and a single decisive checkmate, weiqi is a game of territorial encirclement, in which players gradually surround and absorb the opponent's positions through patient accumulation of positional advantage across an entire board, rather than through any single decisive confrontation. King Lear's opening catastrophe exhibits a structure considerably closer to weiqi's encirclement logic than to chess's direct confrontation. Goneril and Regan do not defeat their father in any single decisive battle of wills in Act One; they instead engage in exactly the kind of patient, incremental erosion of his positional strength — reducing his retinue of knights from one hundred to fifty to a handful to none, across a sequence of escalating demands spread across multiple scenes — that weiqi's own strategic vocabulary would recognize immediately as the gradual conversion of an opponent's apparently strong central position into an effectively surrounded, resourceless one. Lear's own shi, the positional strength his sovereignty had accumulated across a long reign, is not seized from him in a single coup; it is dismantled incrementally, stone by stone, until the board's configuration itself — rather than any single decisive battle — has rendered him powerless. By the time Lear walks into the storm, his shi has already been entirely converted to his daughters' advantage; the heath and the flower crown that follow are not the cause of his collapse but its visible, delayed registration.

26.3  Edmund's Falcon-Strike and Cordelia's Refusal

Edmund's parallel plot against Edgar and Gloucester offers this treatise's clearest illustration of shi's other defining quality: the conversion of minimal force into maximal effect through correct positional timing rather than through raw strength. Edmund, the play's illegitimate and structurally disadvantaged son, possesses none of Lear's accumulated authority and none of Edgar's legitimate inheritance; his entire strategic position, in conventional force-based terms, is weak. And yet Edmund achieves a swifter and more complete reversal of fortune than either Goneril or Regan manages, precisely because he operates with superior command of shi — a forged letter here, a fabricated confession there, each individually modest in its application of force but positioned with the falcon's precise timing Sun Tzu's text invokes, striking exactly when Gloucester's own anxieties about legitimacy and loyalty render him most susceptible to exactly the suggestion Edmund offers.

Cordelia's own catastrophic refusal to play the love-test's rhetorical game in Act One — her famous "Nothing, my lord," repeated and elaborated rather than retracted even as her father's anger escalates — reads, through this part's analytical lens, as a deliberate, principled refusal to deploy shi at all, even when deploying it would have cost her nothing but a few minutes of flattering speech. Where Goneril and Regan demonstrate sophisticated command of exactly the kind of strategic positioning this part has been describing, Cordelia refuses the entire strategic game, insisting on a direct, unconfigured honesty that the play's own logic punishes severely and immediately. Her refusal constitutes the play's most radical political gesture: an explicit rejection of shi's entire logic of positional manipulation in favour of a kind of zero-configuration honesty the play, devastatingly, shows to be catastrophically unsuited to survival within a court already operating by shi's rules.

26.4  The Field as the Board That Will Not Play

It is worth extending this analogy one further step, because the extension brings this treatise's analytical vocabulary back into direct contact with the flower crown that remains its central textual concern. If Act One's court functions as a weiqi board on which Goneril, Regan, and Edmund each successfully accumulate shi against Lear, Edgar, and Gloucester respectively, the corn-field of Act Four functions as a considerably larger, considerably more indifferent board on which no human player's strategic configuration holds any privileged status at all. The field does not encircle Lear the way Goneril's demands encircled him; it simply grows, indiscriminately, whatever conditions of soil and season happen to produce, with the same fumitory, hardocks, and darnel it would produce whether or not any king ever wandered into it. This may be the deepest reason the flower crown so devastatingly completes Lear's specifically strategic catastrophe: having spent the entire play's first half losing a human weiqi match he never recognized himself to be playing, Lear arrives at Act Four only to discover a board — the natural world itself — that does not play weiqi or chess or any game requiring opponents capable of strategic intention at all. The corn-field offers neither encirclement's patient hostility nor chess's direct confrontation; it offers simple, unstrategic abundance, indifferent to king and weed alike, and that absolute absence of any strategic configuration whatsoever may be the more frightening discovery the flower crown stages: that beyond the human court's exhausting strategic warfare lies not a gentler order but no order of intentional opposition at all, merely growth, merely the field's own blind productivity, completely uninterested in whatever battle of shi has just destroyed the kingdom at its edge.

26.5  The Limits of the Analogy

This part closes by registering an honest limitation in extending the weiqi vocabulary this far: the corn-field's indifference is not itself a strategic configuration in any sense Sun Tzu's military philosophy would recognize, and this treatise does not want to claim that classical Chinese strategic thought, developed specifically to analyze contests between intentional human agents, straightforwardly explains a natural order possessing no comparable intentionality at all. What the weiqi analogy supplies, more modestly and more usefully, is a vocabulary for understanding exactly where Lear's catastrophe stops being explicable in shi's terms and starts requiring an entirely different explanatory register — Act One belongs to shi, to the human court's strategic configuration of forces; Act Four belongs to something shi cannot describe at all, a natural order that simply does not play, and the flower crown is the visible seam between these two utterly incompatible registers, sewn directly onto a single human body that must somehow continue to exist, brokenly, across both.

Complexity Coda · XXV

Shì — strategic configuration, the shape of a situation determining which moves become available within it — reads Lear's opening catastrophe not as a single bad decision but as a positional collapse, the love-test changing the entire board's configuration before a single subsequent move is made. This is close to the single most important corrective complex responsive processes theory offers standard change-management practice: that an organisation's "strategy" is never simply a plan some authority chooses and then executes, but a configuration — a shì — continuously being remade by the accumulated local interactions of everyone inside it, such that the same announced plan can land as triumph or catastrophe depending entirely on the configuration it lands into. Sun Tzu's strategist reads the weiqi board's shape before placing a stone, knowing the board's existing configuration constrains what any single stone can accomplish; the complexity-literate change practitioner reads the organisation's existing configuration of relationships and accumulated history the same way, knowing that the identical change announcement, dropped onto a different configuration, will simply not do the same thing twice.

Shi · the field decides the move

Shi — the configuration of forces that determines, before any stone is placed, which moves are even available — is the classical Chinese name for what complexity calls the system's state and what Shaw calls the power figuration: a shape no single player authors and every player is inside. The love-test is a catastrophic misreading of shi. Lear believes he is making a free, sovereign move — dividing the kingdom on terms he sets — when in fact the configuration he has already created (two daughters who have learned that love here means performance, a third who refuses the lesson, a court that rewards fluency over truth) has foreclosed his options before he speaks. He plays as though he stands outside the board, placing stones; he is a stone. The change leader who reads shi knows the announcement will land not as written but as the existing configuration permits — that the same plan dropped onto a different figuration simply does not do the same thing twice.

The amateur plays the move; the master plays the configuration that decides which moves exist.

Sun Tzu's shì and Elias's figuration name, from two entirely separate civilisational starting points, structurally similar objects: a configuration of relative position and power that determines, prior to any single move, what moves are even available to the actors embedded within it. Stacey's own deployment of complexity theory by analogy rather than by direct transposition mirrors the methodological caution both Sun Tzu's strategist and Elias's sociologist share: the weiqi board's shì and the court's figuration are read, not engineered from outside, by an actor who remains, crucially, one more piece on the board rather than a player positioned safely beyond it — which is precisely the position no change-management consultant, however senior, is ever actually able to occupy with respect to the organisation whose shì they have been engaged to read.

XXVI. Cargo Containers for Cornfields: Hopkins, McKellen, and the Militarized Modern-Dress Lear

Part V traced three film adaptations of Lear across three distinct climates and political registers — Kozintsev's, Brook's, and Kurosawa's. This part turns to a more recent and more deliberately contemporary strategy: the late-2010s tradition of screen and stage Lears that pull the play violently into the immediate, recognizable, often dystopian present, raising distinct questions about what happens to the flower-crown motif specifically when the corn-field it requires has been replaced by a cargo container, a militarized checkpoint, or a tent city of the urban homeless.

27.1  Richard Eyre's Militarized London

Richard Eyre's 2018 television film, produced jointly by the BBC and Amazon and starring Anthony Hopkins in a role the actor himself, by his own description, considered "the Everest of acting," relocates the entire play into a highly militarized twenty-first-century Britain functioning, in effect, as a dystopian near-future state — black SUVs, a love-test staged amid the trappings of contemporary political theatre, Lear's eventual exile and madness staged not against an open heath but against a London transformed into something closer to a war zone, complete with checkpoints and the visual grammar of authoritarian crackdown. The production's solution to the hovel scene — Edgar's shelter reimagined not as a rustic Jacobean hovel but as a cargo container within a tent city of the homeless — performs an entirely contemporary translation of the same underlying logic this treatise has traced since its opening parts: a sovereign body, stripped of every marker of authority, must be relocated into whatever materials a given historical moment offers as the visible sign of abjection, and a militarized, unequal twenty-first-century Britain offers, ready to hand, the image of homelessness and improvised shelter that a Jacobean audience would have recognized in straw and hovels.

Significantly, the abridged hundred-and-fifteen-minute running time required Eyre's production to compress and cut substantially, and critical commentary on the film specifically noted that secondary arcs, including material surrounding Edgar's storyline, suffered disproportionately from the cuts — raising, for this treatise's purposes, the question of how the flower-crown scene itself, with its dependence on the prior weed-catalogue and on Edgar's witnessing "side-piercing sight," survives compression of this kind. A production compelled to move at contemporary televisual pace, answerable to an audience's expectations of pacing rather than to the patient, recursive structure of Jacobean dramaturgy, inevitably puts pressure on exactly the kind of slow-building, two-scenes-apart textual architecture this treatise's opening part identified as structurally essential to the scene's original power.

27.2  Casting Age Against the Role

One specific casting decision in Eyre's production deserves attention for what it reveals about how contemporary productions handle the relationship between Lear's own advanced age and the play's broader treatment of aging and bodily decline. Eyre's casting of the Fool as an elderly man, played by Karl Johnson, departed deliberately from the more conventional theatrical practice of casting a younger actor in the role, a choice multiple critics singled out as a particular success, observing that it lent the Fool-Lear relationship a depth of shared history and mutual frailty unavailable when the Fool is played, as is theatrically more common, as a nimble, youthful counterpoint to Lear's aged decline. Anthony Hopkins himself, eighty years old during filming and delivering what he and multiple critics described as a performance whose raw physical vulnerability was inseparable from the actor's own genuine advanced age, represents the culmination of a casting tradition in which the actor's own relationship to age and physical decline becomes part of the production's meaning, the flower-crowned king's collapse reading differently, and arguably more devastatingly, when delivered by a body that is not performing frailty but genuinely possesses it.

27.3  McKellen's Repeated Returns

Ian McKellen's documented history with the role — having first played Lear for the Royal Shakespeare Company in 2007, then returning to a new production in 2017 that was itself revived for a further London run the following year, by which point the actor was seventy-nine — offers different evidence than Hopkins's single, decisively cinematic engagement: a sustained, repeated theatrical relationship with the role across more than a decade, allowing McKellen's interpretation of the flower-crown scene and the broader collapse it culminates to evolve across multiple, separately documented productions rather than crystallizing in a single definitive screen record. This pattern of repeated return — also visible in Antony Sher's reprisal, in his late sixties, of an earlier production — suggests that the role has, for the most senior generation of contemporary British classical actors, become something closer to a recurring vocation than a single career-defining performance, each return offering the opportunity to reconsider, as the actor's own body ages further, exactly how the flower-crowned king's bodily collapse should be staged for an audience increasingly accustomed to seeing the play's full, unsanitized horror.

One widely circulated piece of contemporary commentary accompanying this wave of major productions observed that King Lear had been performed more times in the preceding fifty years than across its entire prior performance history of some three hundred and fifty years, attributing the surge specifically to the play's renewed resonance with "a world of global conflict and a sense of impending apocalypse." This statistic bears directly on this treatise's overall argument: if the play as a whole has found dramatically increased theatrical urgency across exactly the period during which this treatise has documented the flower-crown motif's parallel proliferation across opera, surrealist cinema, clinical-historical scholarship, folklore studies, dance criticism, fashion, and machine-generated imagery, then the motif's recent acceleration reflects a broader cultural recognition that Lear's vision of sovereignty's catastrophic unmaking has simply become more legible, more proximate, more obviously relevant to lived contemporary experience than it was to audiences living through the more settled decades of the mid-twentieth century's postwar order.

27.4  The Risk of the Dystopian Translation

This part closes by registering a genuine interpretive risk that dystopian, militarized-modern-dress Lear productions run, a risk this treatise's parts on theatre and opera did not need to confront in quite the same terms: the danger that translating Lear's world into a recognizable, near-future authoritarian Britain risks converting the play's deep, near-mythic structural logic into a more narrowly topical political allegory about a specific, nameable contemporary anxiety. Some critical responses to Eyre's production registered precisely this discomfort, with at least one reviewer noting that the production's "world makes no sense" as coherent speculative politics even while praising its emotional power. The flower crown itself, notably, becomes one of the most difficult elements to translate convincingly into these militarized-modern settings — a corn-field's weeds do not obviously exist within a checkpoint-and-tent-city visual vocabulary, which is part of why Eyre's production, like several others surveyed in this part, tends to compress or substantially reinterpret the specific imagery of Cordelia's weed-catalogue rather than attempting any literal modern equivalent, relying instead on the broader visual grammar of homelessness and exposure to carry the scene's essential meaning. This treatise takes this consistent compression as further, indirect confirmation of an argument advanced throughout its survey: that the flower crown's specific botanical content matters less than the underlying structural fact it dramatizes, and that contemporary dystopian Lears, even when they cannot find a convincing modern equivalent for fumitory and darnel, remain fully capable of delivering the scene's essential dramaturgical payload through other available materials.

Complexity Coda · XXVI

Hopkins's and McKellen's militarised modern-dress productions relocate the heath's catastrophe onto the iconography of contemporary conflict — cargo containers standing in for cornfields — and in doing so make visible something the play's Jacobean staging could only imply: that Lear's collapse is a collapse of institutional and military command structure as much as of personal sanity. Organisational change literature has its own under-examined debt to military command vocabulary — "deploy," "roll out," "mobilise" — language borrowed from exactly the command-and-control tradition Stacey and Mowles's complex responsive processes theory was developed to argue against. The modern-dress Lear's achievement, on this reading, is to show command language failing in real time, against an enemy and an heath alike indifferent to rank; a change programme that continues to speak the unreconstructed vocabulary of deployment and rollout risks discovering, as Hopkins's Lear discovers among the cargo containers, that the terrain it is operating in no longer honours the chain of command the vocabulary assumes.

Stacey and Mowles devote particular critical attention to the residual military vocabulary still structuring most change-management language — "deploy," "roll out," "mobilise," "command and control" — precisely because this vocabulary smuggles in, beneath its apparently neutral technical surface, an entire Type 2 abstraction about how organisations work: a command issued at the top, executed without modification down a hierarchy, success measured by fidelity to the original order. Hopkins's and McKellen's cargo-container heath stages this vocabulary's failure with unusual visual directness — actual contemporary military hardware, dressed in the iconography of command, proving exactly as indifferent to Lear's authority as Shakespeare's original storm — and offers change-management practice a vivid caution against importing this vocabulary's assumptions about hierarchy and obedience into a figuration that, like Lear's storm-battered company, has long since stopped honouring the chain of command the vocabulary still assumes.

Put the heath in a cargo container and the truth survives the set, because the flower-crown was never a costume but a law — and laws travel.

XXVII. Unaccommodated Man: Bare Life and the Anthropocene Reading of the Heath

Is man no more than this? Consider him well…
Thou art the thing itself: unaccommodated man is no more
but such a poor, bare, forked animal as thou art.
Off, off, you lendings! Come, unbutton here.— King Lear, III.iv — the king strips to the bare creature beneath the costume

This treatise has so far treated the flower crown primarily as an iconographic and performative problem. This part steps back to ask a more directly philosophical question that recent political-philosophical and ecocritical scholarship has pressed with renewed urgency: what does the play's vegetal imagery actually claim about the relationship between sovereignty and the non-human natural world, and why has that claim acquired fresh resonance in an era of climate disruption Shakespeare could not have anticipated but that his text, on a certain reading, seems uncannily to address?

28.1  Unaccommodated Man and the Stripping of Lendings

The textual hinge this reading most consistently returns to precedes the flower-crown scene by roughly two acts, in the storm sequence where Lear, newly encountered with the disguised Edgar's Poor Tom, delivers what remains the play's most philosophically dense meditation on the relationship between humanity and the rest of the natural order. Confronted with Edgar's near-naked, exposed body, Lear asks whether "unaccommodated man is no more but such a poor, bare, forked animal" as the wretch before him, and proceeds, in a gesture critics have long recognized as the play's most radical act of self-stripping, to tear at his own clothes — "off, off, you lendings!" — in an attempt to reduce himself to exactly the same unaccommodated condition. Commentary on this passage has consistently emphasized Lear's reasoning: that clothing represents civilization's "lendings," borrowed trappings that artificially elevate human beings above the animal condition they would otherwise nakedly share, and that stripping away these lendings is Lear's attempt to access some more authentic, unmediated relationship to his own creaturely existence.

What this part wants to add to the iconographic survey conducted elsewhere in this treatise is a reframing of exactly what "nature" means within this gesture, because the reframing complicates any simple reading of the flower crown as merely a continuation of the storm scene's logic of reduction. Paul Cantor's influential reading observes that Lear's stripping gesture seeks to access man's supposed essence by removing every artificial addition civilization has layered onto the bare biological animal — but the flower crown that follows performs something subtly and importantly different: it does not strip Lear down to bare, unaccommodated animality; it re-clothes him, but in nature's own materials rather than civilization's. The movement from Act Three's nakedness to Act Four's flower crown is therefore not simple continuation but a kind of grim joke nature plays on its own prior logic of reduction: having stripped Lear to nothing, it then over-supplies him, indiscriminately, with everything the field happens to be growing, mocking the very dignity-through-minimalism his Act Three gesture had been reaching for.

28.2  Jan Kott's Bleeding Earth, Reconsidered

Part VIII examined Jan Kott's grotesque as a theoretical hinge between tragedy and the absurd; this part returns briefly to Kott's earlier and more directly cosmological formulation, articulated in the same Shakespeare Our Contemporary study, that the play's universe seeks no ultimate meaning or theological consolation at all — that, in Kott's own stark phrase, all that remains by the play's end is "the earth, empty and bleeding," across which the king, the fool, the blind man, and the madman carry on their "distracted dialogue" with no audience beyond each other and no cosmic order listening. This bleak, post-war, distinctly Absurdist-inflected reading has been challenged by subsequent humanist critics, who insist that the play locates a paradoxical dignity precisely within Lear's debasement, that as unaccommodated man he feels what wretches feel, and that the moment of greatest brutalization becomes the moment Lear becomes most fully human, capable for the first time of the compassion his royal insulation had previously made impossible.

This treatise wants to suggest that the flower crown occupies the precise hinge-point between Kott's bleak, meaning-evacuated earth and this humanist counter-reading's redemptive, dignity-through-debasement logic, and that its enduring interpretive richness across the many media surveyed in this treatise's preceding parts stems exactly from its refusal to resolve decisively toward either pole. Read through Kott's lens, the flower crown is simply further evidence of nature's brute indifference — the field does not care that a king wanders into it, supplies him with poison weeds as readily as it would supply any other trespasser, offers no consolation, no theological order, nothing beyond the empty, bleeding ground Kott insists is all the play ultimately provides. Read through the humanist counter-lens, the same crown becomes evidence of the opposite: that it is precisely by being reduced to nature's own undifferentiated material that Lear becomes capable, in the speeches immediately following his entrance, of his most devastating insight into structural injustice. Every artist this treatise has surveyed has effectively had to choose a position somewhere along this same axis, even without citing either critical tradition by name, because the choice is built into the underlying problem itself: is the vegetal world that crowns Lear an indifferent void or a strange, debasing path back toward dignity?

28.3  The Anthropocene Heath

Recent ecocritical scholarship has extended this line of inquiry into explicitly contemporary, climate-conscious territory, reading Lear's storm and heath sequences as an unexpectedly prescient text for an Anthropocene-aware audience increasingly attentive to humanity's own vulnerability before natural forces civilization had assumed, with Lear's own initial confidence in Act One, that it had permanently mastered. Recent ecocritical work explicitly invokes Lear's "unaccommodated man" formulation as a touchstone for reading a documented sense of homelessness amid "the energies of the cosmos" — a homelessness this scholarship links directly to the alienation industrialization has produced, the same alienation now legible in the geological record as the carbon and toxin residue defining the Anthropocene epoch. This scholarship demonstrates that Lear's storm-and-heath sequence has become, for at least one significant strand of contemporary literary-critical practice, a foundational text for thinking about the human species' newly precarious relationship to a natural world it had assumed, across several centuries of industrial confidence, it had safely subordinated.

This treatise wants to register, with appropriate caution, that reading Lear as proto-ecological text risks a certain anachronism the play's own early modern cosmology does not straightforwardly support — Shakespeare's nature is not contemporary ecology's nature, structured by climate science and geological epoch rather than by the four humours and the Great Chain of Being. And yet the underlying structural correspondence this treatise has traced throughout its survey — a sovereign power that assumed mastery over nature, only to discover, through catastrophic personal experience, nature's complete indifference to every hierarchy that sovereign power had imagined permanent — maps with genuine and increasing force onto a contemporary cultural moment in which entire civilizations are discovering, through the catastrophic experience of climate disruption, exactly the same lesson Lear's flower crown dramatizes in miniature: that the field does not recognize the crown, that the corn and the darnel grow indifferently together regardless of which is "sustaining" and which merely infests, and that no amount of accumulated civilizational confidence guarantees the natural world's continued cooperation with human hierarchy.

28.4  Sovereignty Reduced to Bare Life

The broader twentieth-century political-philosophical vocabulary developed around sovereignty and what Giorgio Agamben, building on earlier work by Carl Schmitt and Walter Benjamin, termed "bare life" — the reduction of a political subject to mere biological existence, stripped of the legal and political status that ordinarily protects and confers dignity upon human life within a given order — offers a further, productive frame for understanding why Lear's flower crown has proved so durably resonant across the politically engaged theatre traditions this treatise has surveyed, a resonance already visible in this treatise's discussion of the Royal Conservatoire commission's explicit framing of the weedy crown as available "to anyone of the ninety-nine per cent." Lear's reduction is not merely personal or familial; it is a reduction from sovereign — the figure who, in the classical political-theological tradition Schmitt and Agamben both examine, stands paradoxically both inside and outside the law, the one who can suspend the law's normal operation — to bare life itself, a body the field crowns with the same indifference it would crown any other trespasser, royal or common. This is sovereignty's own self-undoing: the sovereign exception, pushed to its absolute limit, collapses back into the bare biological life it had always, in theory, held the power to except itself from.

28.5  The Field as Neither Villain Nor Redeemer

This part closes by proposing a synthesis the accumulated weight of this treatise's prior survey now licenses: that the corn-field of Act Four, and the flower crown it produces, function in the play's own internal logic as neither simple villain (Kott's bleak, meaningless void) nor simple redeemer (a straightforwardly restorative return to nature's nurturing bosom), but as something more genuinely indifferent and therefore more philosophically demanding than either pole alone can capture — a natural order that confers no inherent meaning on the human distinctions (king versus beggar, sustaining corn versus idle weed) that human society had assumed were themselves natural, while simultaneously, and without contradiction, providing the exact conditions under which something like dignity-through-debasement can briefly, fragilely, emerge. The ecocritical and political-philosophical readings surveyed in this part do not resolve this tension; they sharpen it, and in sharpening it, they explain why every artist this treatise has examined has felt compelled to find their own way of holding both possibilities open at once, refusing the comfort of resolving Lear's weeds into either pure horror or pure consolation.

Complexity Coda · XXVII

The Anthropocene reading of Lear's heath — Agamben's bare life and Schmitt's sovereignty read through a storm that no longer recognises any human claim to special exemption from the natural world's indifference — extends "unaccommodated man" into a register where the entire premise of human institutional control is in question, not merely one king's grip on it. Change-management practice rarely operates at this scale, but the scale shift is instructive: most transformation programmes assume an environment that, while uncertain, remains fundamentally accommodating of organisational will, a stable ground against which the change can be planned. Stacey's edge-of-chaos literature, and the ecological turn in recent organisational scholarship this part surveys, both suggest that the more honest starting assumption, increasingly, is closer to Lear's heath: an environment whose accommodation cannot be taken for granted at any scale, in which organisations are not exempt observers of disruption but, like the unaccommodated king himself, simply one more body exposed to weather, market, and climate systems considerably larger and less negotiable than any single strategy document anticipates.

Dissipative structures · order bought from the storm

Prigogine's deepest finding was that genuine order — the kind that can grow, adapt, surprise itself — arises only in systems held far from equilibrium by continuous exchange with an environment that owes them nothing. Comfort is entropy; the sheltered system, the over-accommodated organisation, winds slowly down toward the heat-death of pure repetition. Lear's “unaccommodated man,” stripped on the heath to “the thing itself,” is a dissipative structure in the instant of its formation: a self that can only emerge once the insulation of the court — its robes, its flatterers, its frozen certainties — has been torn away and the raw weather let in. The Anthropocene reading and the complexity reading converge on a single hard truth: there is no order worth having that does not have to be paid for, continuously, in exposure to a storm that was never on anyone's side.

Comfort is entropy; it is the storm, indifferent and unowned, that makes new order possible.

Prigogine's dissipative structures, the conceptual ancestor of this treatise's edge-of-chaos vocabulary throughout, were developed precisely to explain how genuinely novel order can emerge in systems held far from equilibrium by continuous exchange with an indifferent surrounding environment — a description that fits the Anthropocene reading of Lear's heath with unusual precision. The heath does not accommodate Lear's claim to special exemption from its indifference, any more than a dissipative structure's surrounding thermodynamic environment accommodates the structure's own internal organisation; both persist only by continuous, unprivileged exchange with forces holding no special regard for the structure's survival. An organisation that imagines itself exempt from this condition — assuming a stable, accommodating external environment against which planned change can be safely executed — is, on Prigogine's physics and this part's ecocritical reading alike, simply mistaken about the kind of system it actually is.

XXVIII. Two Lears, Not One: The Quarto/Folio Revision Hypothesis and What It Means for the Flower Scene

We came crying hither:
Thou know’st the first time that we smell the air
We wawl and cry…— King Lear, IV.vi — the text the two versions both keep

This treatise's opening part registered, briefly, that the 1608 Quarto and 1623 Folio texts of King Lear differ at numerous points, but deferred the fuller textual-scholarly story of exactly how those differences have been theorized. That story deserves its own treatment here, because the scholarly consensus on this question changed decisively within living memory, and the change bears directly on how confidently this treatise can speak of "the" flower-crowning scene at all, as opposed to two related but textually distinct scenes belonging to two related but textually distinct plays.

29.1  The Old Consensus: One Lost Original, Two Corrupted Copies

For most of the twentieth century, editorial practice treated the Quarto and Folio texts as imperfect witnesses to a single, lost authorial manuscript, each corrupted in its own way by the processes of transcription, memorial reconstruction, or print-shop error, and the editor's job was accordingly understood to be conflation: combining material from both texts into a single, maximally complete version representing, as nearly as scholarship could reconstruct it, what Shakespeare had actually written. This conflated text — the version most readers encountered for most of the twentieth century, and the version this treatise's own opening parts have largely assumed when discussing "the" weed-catalogue and "the" stage direction — rests on a set of assumptions a subsequent generation of textual scholars would directly challenge: that the differences between Quarto and Folio do not indicate any deliberate rewriting by the author, that Shakespeare wrote only one original manuscript now lost, and that both surviving printed texts contain various distortions of that single lost original.

29.2  The Revision Hypothesis: Two Authorial Versions

Beginning with Madeleine Doran's comparatively isolated 1931 suggestion that the two texts had independent histories worth taking seriously in their own right, and revived with much greater force in the late 1970s and early 1980s by Michael Warren and Steven Urkowitz, a rival hypothesis gained increasing scholarly traction: that the Quarto and the Folio are not two corrupted witnesses to one lost original but two distinct, internally coherent, separately authorial versions of the play, with the Folio representing Shakespeare's own later revision of the earlier Quarto text. This argument reached its most influential and comprehensive statement in Gary Taylor and Michael Warren's 1983 edited collection The Division of the Kingdoms: Shakespeare's Two Versions of King Lear, a book a later academic conference would credit with provoking a broader "crisis in editing" across Shakespearean textual scholarship generally, and which Stanley Wells and Gary Taylor's subsequent Oxford Shakespeare made institutionally consequential by printing the Quarto and Folio as two separate, individually edited texts rather than as a single conflated whole — the first major edition to do so.

The scale of the textual differences these scholars were arguing deserved to be taken seriously as authorial revision rather than mere corruption is considerable. The Folio cuts approximately three hundred lines found in the Quarto while adding roughly one hundred lines not found there, alongside several hundred further lexical substitutions — one frequently cited tally puts the number of individual verbal changes at eight hundred and fifty. Crucially for the revision hypothesis's persuasiveness, these differences do not fall evenly across the text, as undirected scribal or compositorial error would predict; they cluster with suspicious consistency around specific characters and specific scenes. Steven Urkowitz's influential analysis of the Duke of Albany found the character rendered markedly less sympathetic in the Folio than in the Quarto, more visibly torn between his official duty as the invaded land's ruler and his private sympathy for the deposed Lear — a clustering of difference around one character's moral coherence that a theory of random corruption struggles to explain, but that a theory of deliberate authorial reconsideration explains easily.

29.3  The Two Fools: Artificial Wit Against Natural Pathos

Of the many character-specific differences textual scholars have catalogued between the two versions, one bears with unusual directness on this treatise's central textual concern, because it concerns the very character — the Fool — whose absence from Nahum Tate's later adaptation this treatise has already examined at length, and whose presence the original flower-crowning scene's tonal logic depends upon. Scholarship comparing the Fool's role across the two texts has identified a consistent characterological shift: the Quarto's Fool embodies something closer to the "artificial fool" type, characterized by sharper wit and a more bitter, more deliberately cutting comic edge, while the Folio's revisions produce something closer to a "natural fool," a more purely pathetic figure whose comedy has been softened in favour of pathos. This same scholarship has proposed that the Folio's various excisions of some of the Fool's more politically pointed material may reflect contemporary censorship pressure, whether externally imposed or self-imposed by a playwright and company increasingly attentive to how directly topical political commentary could be permitted to land on the early Stuart stage — a possibility that would mean the Folio's somewhat gentler, more purely pitiable Fool is not simply an aesthetic refinement but a politically motivated softening, made necessary by the same kind of pressure this treatise's account of George III's reign showed operating with much greater and more explicit force two centuries later.

This finding bears directly on how confidently this treatise can describe the flower-crowning scene's governing tonal paradox — the simultaneous holding-together of pity and political clairvoyance this treatise's opening parts identified as central to the scene's power. If the Quarto's sharper-witted, more bitingly satirical Fool establishes a different baseline tone across the play's first three acts than the Folio's gentler, more purely pathetic version, then Lear's own later flower-crowned assumption of the Fool's truth-telling function — his transformation, in effect, into his own Fool once the licensed jester's role has nowhere else to migrate — inherits a subtly different character depending on which text a given reader, editor, or director is working from. A Quarto-based staging inherits a sharper, more cutting comic register that Lear's later mad speeches can be played to recall; a Folio-based staging inherits a gentler, more purely pitiable one. This treatise's own preceding survey, like most modern popular engagement with the play, has necessarily worked primarily from conflated or Folio-leaning modern editions, and this part wants to flag, with appropriate scholarly honesty, that a reader working instead from the Quarto's distinct textual tradition would encounter a flower-crowning scene inflected by a meaningfully different prior characterization of exactly the figure — the Fool — whose absence or presence this treatise has shown to carry such disproportionate interpretive weight.

29.4  Skeptical Resistance and the Persistence of the Debate

The revision hypothesis did not achieve uncontested scholarly victory, and this treatise's commitment to intellectual honesty requires registering the considerable body of skeptical response it has generated across the decades since 1983. A consistent line of objection, advanced across numerous scholarly interventions through the 1980s, 1990s, and into the 2000s, holds that if Shakespeare had genuinely wanted to revise the play as substantially as the theory proposes — reshaping Albany's and Edgar's characters, reconceiving the play's treatment of the climactic war — he would most likely have made more extensive, more obviously deliberate changes than the comparatively modest, line-level differences actually observed between the two texts. More recent computational approaches to the question, applying statistical and stylometric methods to the pattern of variants, have continued to probe the dispute without producing a final, universally accepted resolution, meaning that more than four decades after Taylor and Warren's original intervention, the question of whether King Lear exists as one play with two corrupted witnesses or two plays with one shared origin remains, properly speaking, unsettled rather than closed.

29.5  What This Means for "The" Flower Scene

This part closes by drawing out the implication this unsettled textual history holds for this treatise's entire preceding survey, an implication this treatise wants to state plainly rather than allow to remain merely implicit. When this document refers, as it has done throughout, to "the" flower-crowning scene, "the" stage direction, "the" weed-catalogue, it is making a simplifying editorial choice that the field of Shakespearean textual scholarship itself no longer makes without qualification. Modern scholarly editions increasingly present, as the Internet Shakespeare Editions and the New Cambridge Shakespeare both now do, two distinct texts rather than one conflated whole, each with its own internally coherent characterization, its own slightly different Fool, its own slightly different distribution of political weight across the play's final two acts. The flower-crowned king who walks onstage in the Quarto's "fields neere Dover" is not, strictly, identical in every textual particular to the flower-crowned king who walks onstage in the Folio's equivalent scene, even though both are recognizably the same dramatic event, the same image, the same twelve-word stage direction's worth of theatrical instruction. This treatise has, throughout, followed the conflated and Folio-leaning convention most modern popular and theatrical engagement with the play continues to follow, and considers this a defensible simplification for a study concerned primarily with the image's cross-medial afterlife rather than with the fine grain of its textual transmission — but it registers, in closing this part, that even the originary textual ground this entire study has been built upon is, on its own terms, considerably less stable, less singular, than the phrase "Shakespeare's stage direction" comfortably implies.

Complexity Coda · XXVIII

The Quarto/Folio revision hypothesis — that Shakespeare himself returned to and reworked his own play, producing two textually distinct versions rather than one corrupted by transmission error — offers a useful model for thinking about organisational change as genuinely iterative rather than singly authored and then simply executed. Most change-management methodology still imagines a single authoritative "version" of the transformation plan, departures from which are treated as drift to be corrected back toward the original text. Stacey's complex responsive processes tradition, and the scholarly consensus this part surveys around Shakespeare's own apparent willingness to revise his most punishing tragedy after its first performance, both suggest the more accurate and more productive model is closer to the dual-text tradition itself: not one authoritative plan corrupted by imperfect execution, but successive, legitimately differing versions, each responding to what was actually learned in performance, with no obligation to treat the earlier draft as more authentic than the revision it provoked.

Stacey's account of organisational learning as continuous, iterative revision rather than singular authorial pronouncement finds its most direct literary-historical parallel in the Quarto/Folio revision hypothesis this part surveys: Shakespeare's own apparent willingness to return to and substantially rework his most punishing tragedy after its first performance is, on this reading, exactly the iterative, experience-responsive revision Stacey's Type 1 abstraction recommends and most change-management methodology's single authoritative "version one" of the transformation plan forecloses. The scholarly refusal to rank Quarto and Folio as authentic original versus corrupted copy, treating both instead as legitimately differing responses to what was learned in intervening performance, supplies change management with its own missing permission: to treat a revised change plan not as a confession of the original's failure but as the same kind of legitimate, experience-informed second version Shakespeare's own editorial history records.

Two texts of one play, and the flower scene unstable between them: even the words of the crown will not hold still, because the thing they describe is change itself.

XXIX. Before Shakespeare's Pen: Monmouth, Holinshed, and a Real Daughter Named Cordell

Part XIV examined what happened to Lear's flower-crowned madness after Nahum Tate restored the story's older, happier ending to the English stage in 1681. This part reaches further back, to ask what the story looked like before Shakespeare ever touched it, because the deep textual prehistory of King Lear reveals something this treatise's argument needs registered plainly: in every documented version of this story that predates Shakespeare's own, Lear does not go mad at all, and there is no flower crown for any later artist to inherit. Madness, and the vegetal imagery through which Shakespeare chose to stage it, are not legendary inheritances. They are Shakespeare's own invention, grafted onto source material that, for nearly five centuries before he wrote, had told this story without them.

30.1  Geoffrey of Monmouth's Happy King

The earliest recorded version of the legend underlying King Lear appears in Geoffrey of Monmouth's Historia Regum Britanniae, a twelfth-century Latin chronicle blending purported British history with mythic embellishment. In Monmouth's account, Leir succeeds his father as ruler of Britain, reigns for sixty years, founds the city of Kaerleir — the Leicester of later English geography — and, lacking male heirs, divides his realm among his three daughters according to their professions of love for him, exactly as Shakespeare's opening scene would later stage. But Monmouth's Leir does not go mad, does not wander any heath, and does not die in despair: Cordelia, in this earliest version, defeats her treacherous elder sisters, restores her father to the throne, and succeeds him as ruler in her own right after his peaceful death. The entire architecture this treatise has spent its preceding parts examining — madness, the storm, the flower crown, the catastrophic deaths of both Lear and Cordelia — exists nowhere in the story's original twelfth-century form.

30.2  Holinshed's Chronicle and the Anonymous Leir Play

Shakespeare's most immediate sources compound rather than complicate this absence. Raphael Holinshed's Chronicles of England, Scotlande, and Irelande, in its 1587 second edition, transmitted Monmouth's account largely unchanged in its essential structure, dating the legendary events to the era of the biblical King Joash of Judah and preserving the same restorative ending. And the anonymous play most scholars agree Shakespeare drew on more directly than any other single source, The True Chronicle History of King Leir and His Three Daughters, registered with the Stationers' Company in 1594 and printed in 1605 — close enough to Shakespeare's own composition date that scholars debate whether the printing was timed to capitalize on the success of Shakespeare's already-circulating play, or whether Shakespeare's own interest in the subject was instead sparked by seeing the older play newly available in print — likewise ends happily, with Leir restored and reconciled, the elder daughters' treachery exposed and punished, no madness scene of any kind required by the plot's comic, redemptive architecture.

What Shakespeare did to this inherited material, on the evidence this part has assembled, is therefore considerably more radical than simple adaptation. He took a story whose every prior version moved toward restoration and reconciliation, and rewrote its ending toward catastrophe — killing Cordelia, a death no source he is known to have used contains, and that critics have long recognized as Shakespeare's single most consequential departure from everything that came before him. And it was specifically in service of this darker, deliberately un-redemptive trajectory that Shakespeare appears to have invented, wholesale, the elements this treatise's entire survey has been devoted to: the Fool, who exists in none of the sources; the Earl of Kent's loyal disguise, elaborated from a comparatively minor source character; and Lear's madness itself, which no extant pre-Shakespearean version of this story stages at all. The flower crown, in other words, is not a legendary inheritance Shakespeare merely costumed more vividly than his sources. It is a wholly original addition, invented specifically to carry the weight of a tragic trajectory Shakespeare himself imposed on material that had, for four and a half centuries before him, insisted on a happy ending.

30.3  The Annesley Case: A Real Cordell, 1603

One further piece of this prehistory deserves attention because it suggests Shakespeare's darkening of the story may have been catalyzed not only by literary ambition but by a real, contemporary, and widely discussed legal scandal. In 1603, an elderly former courtier of Queen Elizabeth named Sir Brian Annesley, a wealthy Kentish gentleman with three daughters, became the subject of a legal dispute startlingly close to the Lear story's own central premise: his eldest daughter, Grace, attempted to have her aging father declared legally insane so that she and her husband could seize control of his estate, while his youngest, unmarried daughter — whose name, astonishingly, was Cordell — successfully defended her father in court against her sister's petition. One of the executors named in Annesley's eventual will was Sir William Hervey, a documented associate of the family of Shakespeare's own patron, the Earl of Southampton, placing the playwright within a social network where the case's details would very plausibly have circulated as topical gossip well before any literary use was made of them.

This treatise wants to register the Annesley case's significance carefully, without overstating what can be claimed from a coincidence of names and circumstances alone. No direct documentary evidence proves Shakespeare drew on the Annesley affair specifically, and the broader scholarly consensus continues to treat Monmouth, Holinshed, and the anonymous Leir play as the demonstrable textual sources, with the Annesley case offered as a plausible contemporary resonance rather than a confirmed source. But the case's existence does something useful for this treatise's argument regardless of direct causal proof: it confirms that the question Shakespeare's play poses — what happens to an aging patriarch's authority, dignity, and legal personhood when his own children move to have him declared incompetent — was not, for a 1603-to-1606 London audience, a purely antiquarian or legendary concern borrowed from a twelfth-century Latin chronicle. It was a live, locally circulating, contemporary anxiety about aging, inheritance, and the precise legal and social mechanisms by which a father's reason could be officially stripped from him by his own family — precisely the mechanism Goneril and Regan deploy against Lear, dressed in the vocabulary of "idle old man" rather than formal legal incompetency proceedings, but recognizable, to an audience that had just watched a real Cordell defend a real elderly father against a real such proceeding, as the same underlying social drama.

30.4  What This Means for the Flower Crown's Originality

This part closes by drawing out the implication this textual prehistory holds for the entire survey conducted across this treatise's preceding parts. Every later artist examined in this document — Millais painting Ophelia, Reimann composing for Bo Skovhus's baritone, Kurosawa relocating the catastrophe to Sengoku Japan, Švankmajer animating a decomposing vegetable woman, the Royal Conservatoire's florist choosing dying materials for a 2015 commission — has been responding to, reworking, or independently rediscovering a structural device that Shakespeare himself invented rather than inherited. The flower crown carries no legendary weight reaching back to Geoffrey of Monmouth's twelfth century; it carries only the weight Shakespeare himself gave it, in a single, historically specific act of dramaturgical invention that took a four-and-a-half-century-old story of restoration and forced it, against every prior version's own narrative logic, toward catastrophe. This treatise's repeated finding — that artists across wildly disparate media and centuries keep independently rediscovering the same underlying structure — is, on this reading, evidence not of some ancient archetype Shakespeare merely transcribed from deep cultural memory, but of the sheer durability of one playwright's specific, datable, original choice: to let an aging king lose not just his throne but his mind, and to show that loss to his audience dressed in nothing but the field's own indifferent abundance.

30.5  A Note on the Name Itself

One further, smaller piece of this prehistory deserves brief mention, because it connects directly to this treatise's closing discussion of Edmund Spenser's faculty-psychology allegory. Spenser's own Faerie Queene, published in the 1590s, contains a character named Cordelia who, like Shakespeare's, dies by hanging — a detail several scholars have proposed as a direct or indirect source for Shakespeare's own choice of name and fate, distinct from the Cordelia of Monmouth, Holinshed, and the anonymous Leir play, all of whom, this part has already shown, survive and inherit the throne. If this proposed line of influence holds, it means Shakespeare drew the name and the manner of one major character's death from the very poet whose allegorical House of Temperance, examined in this treatise's penultimate part, supplied this treatise's clearest independent confirmation of "fantastically" own diagnostic meaning — which would mean Spenser's imprint on King Lear runs deeper than a single shared vocabulary of faculty psychology, reaching into the play's most devastating plot choice as well. This treatise does not want to overstate a connection the documentary record cannot fully settle, but it notes, in closing, that if Spenser indeed supplied both the name of Shakespeare's most innocent victim and the allegorical vocabulary for diagnosing her father's collapse, then two of this treatise's separate lines of inquiry — the play's sources and the play's diagnostic language — converge on a single Elizabethan poet considerably more central to King Lear's creation than his absence from most modern stagings would suggest.

Complexity Coda · XXIX

The pre-Shakespearean chronicle tradition — Monmouth, Holinshed, the anonymous True Chronicle History, even the documented 1603 Annesley case — establishes that Shakespeare inherited a story already centuries old and changed its ending, inventing the catastrophe modern audiences now take to be the story's only possible shape. This part's finding has a direct change-management application: organisations inherit their own chronicle traditions, accumulated institutional folklore about how change has always gone here, and these inherited narratives constrain what any new change initiative can plausibly claim about its own likely ending every bit as much as the older Leir chronicles constrained, without determining, what Shakespeare's audience expected from his retelling. Stacey's attention to organisational history as a living constraint on present interaction, not a closed and finished past, suggests that a change practitioner ignorant of an organisation's own Holinshed — its accumulated folklore of past restructurings, past promises kept or broken — will misjudge, as badly as a director ignorant of the older Leir chronicles would misjudge Shakespeare's departure from them, just how startling and how risky the proposed new ending actually is.

Elias's processual sociology insists that no figuration is ever encountered fresh; every present configuration of power and relationship carries, sedimented within it, the accumulated residue of long developmental processes stretching back well beyond any participant's living memory. The pre-Shakespearean chronicle tradition this part surveys — Monmouth, Holinshed, the older Leir story Shakespeare inherited and rewrote — is this treatise's clearest demonstration of exactly this Eliasian sedimentation operating on a single narrative across centuries. An organisation's own accumulated institutional folklore about how change has always gone here performs the identical sedimenting function, and a change practitioner who treats the organisation as a blank chronicle awaiting a first telling, rather than as a figuration already centuries deep in its own Holinshed, will misjudge the proposed new ending's actual risk and actual novelty as badly as a director ignorant of the older Leir tradition would misjudge Shakespeare's own departure from it.

Shakespeare inherited the king and invented the flowers; the sources gave him a fall, and he gave the fall its crown of weeds.
Complexity Coda · XXIX

Shakespeare inherited from Holinshed and the Monmouth chronicle a Lear who is restored — a king who regains his throne and dies in peace — and made the single most consequential change in the play’s history: he refused the restoration and added the flowers. The sources gave him an equilibrium story (order lost, order regained); he wrote a far-from-equilibrium one (order lost, and something stranger and more alive grown in its place). This is the treatise’s thesis embedded in the play’s own composition history: Shakespeare took a tale of the machine metaphor — the kingdom as a mechanism that breaks and is repaired — and rewrote it as a study in emergence, crowning his king not with the recovered gold of the chronicles but with the wild, unauthored, self-organising weeds of the field.

XXX. Birdcages and Broken Violins: Peter Maxwell Davies's Other Mad King

Part VI traced Reimann's operatic Lear at length; this part turns to a different, smaller-scaled, and in some respects even more theatrically extreme work of musical madness, one that depicts not Shakespeare's legendary Lear but the same historical monarch whose own actual derangement, this treatise's part on Nahum Tate's long suppression of the play already showed, made Shakespeare's fictional mad king too dangerous to stage in London for nearly a decade. Peter Maxwell Davies's Eight Songs for a Mad King, composed in 1969, completes a circle this treatise has been tracing since its account of George III's reign: the real mad king whose existence once made the fictional one unstageable has, in this later work, become the explicit subject of music theatre in his own right.

31.1  A Mechanical Organ and a Vision of Birdsong

The work's genesis, by the composer's and librettist's own account, began with an object rather than an idea: in 1966 the writer Randolph Stow was shown a miniature mechanical organ that had belonged to George III, and the encounter produced an image Stow himself later described in vivid terms — he imagined the King, in his purple dressing-gown and ermine night-cap, struggling to teach birds to make music, attempting to sing along with them in a voice made almost inhuman by day-long soliloquies. This single image generated the entire piece's theatrical premise: George III, in his historically documented later years, did attempt to teach his pet bullfinches to sing, and Stow's libretto, drawing on contemporaneous documentation of the King's actual outbursts recorded by the royal housekeeper Fanny Burney, stages the monarch's eight monologues as a one-sided conversation with birds represented, in performance, by live instrumentalists traditionally seated within giant birdcages onstage — flute, clarinet, violin, and cello standing in for the bullfinches, with the percussionist alone representing the King's human keeper.

31.2  A Voice Built for Madness

The piece was composed specifically for the South African actor and vocal pioneer Roy Hart, whose documented vocal range exceeded five octaves and who had developed, in his own pedagogical practice, an extraordinary command of extended techniques — multiphonics, the simultaneous sounding of multiple pitches within a single human voice, dramatic vocal distortions exceeding anything the conventional operatic baritone repertoire required. This treatise's part on Reimann's Lear already established that the operatic mad-scene tradition built for baritone voice required a wholly new vocabulary distinct from the soprano coloratura convention; Maxwell Davies's work, composed nearly a decade before Reimann's premiere, pushes considerably further still, demanding a vocal instrument capable of registering madness not through dissonant orchestration around a relatively conventional vocal line but through the voice's own internal fragmentation — a single performer's body required to sound, at moments, like more than one voice simultaneously, a literalization of the divided, multiplying selfhood that this treatise's entire survey has identified as the underlying condition the flower-crowned and weed-crowned mad king embodies.

31.3  The Broken Violin and the Murder of a Part of the Self

The work's climax, arriving at the close of its seventh song, performs in literal stagecraft a version of the symbolic self-violence this treatise has traced repeatedly across its survey of the flower-crowned motif. The King, having begun the song with a direct musical quotation of Handel's "Comfort ye" from Messiah — a piece historically documented as one of the real George III's personal favourites — descends into an off-kilter foxtrot distortion of the same material before erupting into violence, snatching the violin from its birdcage and smashing it. Maxwell Davies's own description of this moment, preserved in the work's performance documentation, names the act with unusual directness as a giving-in to insanity, and the ritual murder by the King of a part of himself — an explicit acknowledgment that the violin-bird, destroyed at this climactic moment, functions not as an external object but as an externalized fragment of the King's own fracturing identity, exactly as this treatise has argued the flower crown functions for Lear: not ornament applied from outside but a visible, material extension of an interior collapse that has nowhere else to register itself.

Rhythmanalysis · the broken time of the mad king

Maxwell Davies stages madness as shattered rhythm — Handel's stately "Comfort ye" buckling into an off-kilter foxtrot before the violin is smashed — and the Mowles volume supplies the conceptual frame that makes this more than coincidence. In her chapter, Rikke Horup adapts Henri Lefebvre's rhythmanalysis to leadership, arguing that organisational life is constituted by interlocking rhythms of time and space, and that the felt experience of leadership — including the alienation and stuckness leaders report — is fundamentally a matter of rhythm: of whose tempo governs, of where the polyrhythms clash, of when a shared cadence breaks down. Crucially, Horup finds that "in seemingly stuck and alienating rhythms, we might find degrees of freedom" — that attending to the broken or oppressive rhythm is itself the route to a different one. The mad king's shattered foxtrot is rhythmanalysis made audible: sovereignty is a rhythm (the measured cadence of ceremony, the stately tempo of "Comfort ye"), and madness is that rhythm breaking — the same break Lear's verse performs when it buckles into the prose of the heath. An organisation, too, has a sovereign rhythm, and a change is felt first as a disturbance of tempo before it is understood as anything else. The leader who listens, as Horup's rhythmanalyst listens, for where the shared cadence is breaking — rather than insisting on the old measure — is the one who finds, in the broken rhythm, the degree of freedom the new pattern needs to form.

Sovereignty is a cadence and madness is its breaking; change is heard as a disturbance of tempo long before it is understood as anything at all.

The work's final words, as the King contemplates his own death while the percussionist follows him offstage beating time, dissolve into a repeated, desperate invocation that this treatise cannot help but hear as a direct, if almost certainly unintended, echo of Shakespeare's own ending: "he will die howling, howling, howling," fading as the performer exits. Lear's own final act, examined in this treatise's discussion of Reimann's operatic setting, builds toward an identical repeated cry — four "no"s, five "never"s, and, in the play's stage direction, a final wordless howl over Cordelia's body — and the structural correspondence between Shakespeare's reduction of articulate kingship to repeated, animal-register sound and Stow's and Maxwell Davies's identical reduction of George III's own historical madness to the same bare repeated word suggests that this particular vocal gesture — the collapse of royal language into a single, repeated, pre-linguistic cry — constitutes one of the most stable and recurring formal solutions this entire treatise has located for representing sovereignty's terminal unmaking, available independently to a Jacobean playwright and a twentieth-century avant-garde composer working on entirely separate historical kings three and a half centuries apart.

31.4  Reception as Scandal, Reception as Recognition

The work's 1969 premiere at London's Queen Elizabeth Hall produced a documented mixture of enthusiasm and outrage characteristic of the broader avant-garde theatrical moment this treatise's discussion of Peter Brook's Marat/Sade has already touched on — contemporary accounts record boos, heckling, and walkouts alongside the work's evidently considerable impact on at least one audience member who would go on to a significant career of his own, the twenty-two-year-old David Bowie, present at the original performance.
