  25. Qu Yuan (屈原), the Li Sao and the broader Chu Ci anthology, with reference to Sima Qian's Records of the Grand Historian and the Dragon Boat Festival tradition (Part XVIII).
  26. The Zhuangzi and Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy scholarship on its reception within Chinese intellectual history (Part XII).
  27. Sun Tzu's Art of War and scholarship on the concept of shi (勢) and the game of weiqi/Go in Chinese strategic thought, including David Lai's monograph for the U.S. Army War College (Part XXV).
  28. Library of Congress Folklife Today blog series and other folklore scholarship on the Green Man, Jack-in-the-Green, the Wild Man/woodwose tradition, and Lady Raglan's 1939 Folklore article (Part XXII).
  29. Court jester and motley costume history, Robert Armin's Foole upon Foole (1605), and scholarship on the fool's role in Shakespeare's company (Part XXXV).
  30. Edmund Spenser, The Faerie Queene, Book II, the House of Temperance and the personification of Phantastes; Shakespeare, A Midsummer Night's Dream, Theseus's speech on "the lunatic, the lover, and the poet" (Part XXXVI).
  31. Scott McCloud's theory of comics "closure," applied to graphic-novel adaptations of King Lear, including work by Ian Pollock and Gareth Hinds (Part X).
  32. Richard Eyre's 2018 King Lear (BBC/Amazon) with Anthony Hopkins; Ian McKellen's repeated stage productions; contemporary reviews and reception documenting the surge in twenty-first-century Lear productions (Part XXVI).
  33. Royal Conservatoire of Scotland's 2015 all-female King Lear and its floral-crown commission, with Jennifer Hamilton's published reading of the weedy crown as anti-hierarchical symbol (Part XVI).
  34. Generative AI image synthesis (Midjourney, Stable Diffusion, DALL-E) and documented prompt-engineering conventions for floral and "fantastical" imagery (Part XIII).
  35. Giorgio Agamben, Carl Schmitt, and Walter Benjamin on sovereignty and "bare life"; recent ecocritical and Anthropocene-aware scholarship on King Lear's storm and heath sequences, including work extending the "unaccommodated man" formulation to later writers (Part XXVII).
  36. HBO's Succession and its widely discussed critical reception as a contemporary Lear analogue (Part XXXVII).
  37. Ralph D. Stacey and Chris Mowles, Strategic Management and Organisational Dynamics, 7th ed. (Pearson, 2016), and the wider theory of complex responsive processes developed at the University of Hertfordshire's Complexity and Management Centre; Chris Mowles, Rethinking Management: Radical Insights from the Complexity Sciences (Gower, 2011) and Managing in Uncertainty: Complexity and the Paradoxes of Everyday Organisational Life (Routledge, 2015) (Complexity Codas, all Parts). The 7th edition is drawn on directly in Part Ie for Mead's concept of cult values and their functionalisation, the misreading of the Boids simulation, and Elias's account of the dynamics of inclusion–exclusion, gossip, and ideology; in Part Ib for Ashby's Law of Requisite Variety (only variety can absorb variety); and in Part III for the "avalanches of destruction… with many small and few large extinction events" that mark a system poised at self-organised criticality (Per Bak's sandpile).
  38. Douglas Griffin and Ralph D. Stacey (eds.), Complexity and the Experience of Leading Organizations (Routledge, 2005), and within it Douglas Griffin's account (after his The Emergence of Leadership, 2002) of cult values, the cult leader, and the potential failure of ethics — the tendency, under conditions of anxiety, for the idealisation of a leader to generate unrealistic expectations that, when disappointed, flip into denigration ("the good and the bad will emerge at the same time"), at the extreme producing "fascist power structures"; and the contrast between participative self-organisation and the mainstream fantasy of the leader who "stands outside the system, designing, manipulating variables and pulling levers" (Part Ie).
  39. Chris Mowles, Emma Crewe and Kiran Chauhan (eds.), Complexity and Leadership (Routledge, 2022), and within it: Keven Bartle's chapter on Hannah Arendt's promising and forgiving as the two repairs for the irreversibility and unpredictability of action, and on the three myths of transformational leadership (enduring harmony, positional authority, complexity reduction) as instances of Elias's "magico-mythical thinking"; Kevin Flinn's chapter on experiential groups and his account of idealised models as relievers of leaders' anxiety (Flinn, 2011: 128); and Rikke Horup's chapter adapting Lefebvre's rhythmanalysis to leadership (Part Id, Parts XIII, XXX).
  40. Şefika Şule Erçetin and Şuay Nilhan Açıkalın (eds.), Chaos, Complexity and Leadership 2018 (Springer Proceedings in Complexity, 2020), and within it: Halil İbrahim Özmen's "The Achilles' Heel of Strategic Management," mapping the modernist strategic-leadership control fantasy onto the tragedy of Achilles and naming the chaos-theoretic phases of strategic change (entropy, bifurcation, metamorphosis, evaluation, stabilisation); and M. Şahin Bülbül's "Artificial Intelligence as a Strange Attractor of the Future," on the branching/bifurcating structure of the future and the machine's holistic, distinction-free perception (Part Id, Part XIII).
  41. Kevin Flinn, A Web of Relations, and the facilitation and complexity-informed organisational development practice it gathers, on the conduct of organisational change as a continuously negotiated, locally enacted process rather than a centrally designed and installed one (Complexity Codas, all Parts).
  42. Ilya Prigogine's Nobel-recognised work on dissipative structures and self-organisation far from thermodynamic equilibrium, including Order Out of Chaos (with Isabelle Stengers, 1984), as the physical-scientific grounding for the edge-of-chaos literature applied throughout the Complexity Codas; Russ Marion, The Edge of Organization: Chaos and Complexity Theories of Formal Social Systems (1999), and the broader literature on complexity theory and organisational change surveyed across the Codas.
  43. Kurt Lewin's three-step "unfreeze, change, refreeze" model of planned change, and John Kotter's eight-step change model, examined throughout the Complexity Codas as the planned, linear paradigm against which the complex responsive processes critique is developed, together with subsequent scholarly reappraisal of Lewin's own legacy (Cummings, Bridgman, and Brown, 2016).
  44. Norbert Elias's processual sociology and his concept of the figuration, including The Court Society (Collected Works, Vol. 2) and Involvement and Detachment (Collected Works, Vol. 8), as taken up directly by Stacey into the theory of complex responsive processes; George Herbert Mead, Mind, Self, and Society (1934), on the gesture-response structure of the social act, as the pragmatist pillar of the same theory (Part Ia and Complexity Codas throughout).
  45. Philip Streatfield, The Paradox of Control in Organizations (Routledge, 2001), on the simultaneous experience of being in control and not in control of organisational outcomes; Hannah Arendt's concept of natality, as invoked by Stacey in his own closing public exposition of complex responsive processes theory in 2018 (Part Ia).
  46. Caroline Halpin and Philomena Hanlon, "Interaction of the Legitimate System and the Shadow System in Organisations," and the broader Staceyan literature on the legitimate system, the shadow system, and bounded instability as the productive condition for organisational creativity and innovation (Part Ia).
  47. Patricia Shaw, Changing Conversations in Organizations: A Complexity Approach to Change (Routledge, 2002), on conversation as the activity in which organisations are continuously sustained and changed, on self-organising power figurations after Elias, on emergence at the edge of chaos, on the impossibility of the "programmer" who would set the conditions of human interaction from outside it, on the Catch-22 by which the most uncertain situations most need and least legitimate open conversation, and on the "seed of a new pattern forming as the previous one dissolved," located not in any single word but in the dispersal itself (Parts II, Ib, Ic, and Complexity Codas throughout).
  48. Patricia Shaw and Ralph Stacey (eds.), Experiencing Risk, Spontaneity and Improvisation in Organizational Change: Working Live (Routledge, 2006), including the contributions on Keith Johnstone's theatre improvisation, the "bench" exercise, status transactions, accepting the offer, and the linking of power and spontaneity in organisational change as ensemble improvisation (Part Ib).
  49. Elizabeth McMillan, Complexity, Management and the Dynamics of Change: Challenges for Practice (Routledge, 2008), on the edge of chaos as the zone where a system's parts "never quite lock into place and yet never quite break up," on Kauffman's ice/water/steam analogy, on complex adaptive systems, self-organisation, fractals, and the Fractal Web and Edge of Chaos Assessment models (Part Ib and Complexity Codas); and, in the botanical edge notes, on the Fibonacci petal-counts of flowers (Part XIX), on co-evolution and parasitism including the foxglove and the parasite that benefits at its host's expense (Part XXI), and on self-organising systems as self-renewing systems that "dissipate their energy in order to renew or recreate themselves" (Parts XXII, XI). McMillan is also drawn on for Tim Smit's Lost Gardens of Heligan and the Eden Project — "the only rainforest in captivity," built in a disused china clay pit on a management philosophy "based not on notions of control but on openness" (Part XII), and, in Part III, for the reading of Lear's weed-crown as the pioneer species of ecological succession on disturbed ground.
  50. Paul Aitken and Malcolm Higgs, Developing Change Leaders: The Principles and Practices of Change Leadership Development (Butterworth-Heinemann/Routledge, 2010), and Malcolm Higgs and David Rowland's empirical research distinguishing Directive, Master, Self-Assembly, and Emergent change approaches, finding the Emergent approach (change "with" rather than "to" people; change that can "start anywhere in the system") most effective in high-magnitude change and most descriptive of "how change actually happens as opposed to how change is articulated" (Part Ib and Complexity Codas); and on Aitken and Higgs's concept of "Change Presence," glossed from family-systems theory as "a non-anxious presence in a sea of anxiety" (Part Ia).
  51. Esther Cameron and Mike Green, Making Sense of Change Management: A Complete Guide to the Models, Tools and Techniques of Organizational Change, 6th ed. (Kogan Page, 2024), drawn on for Gareth Morgan's organizational metaphors — machine, political system, organism, and flux and transformation — and Morgan's principle that "in complex systems no one is ever in a position to control or design system operations… Form emerges. It cannot be imposed" (Part Ia); for the regenerative-ecosystem principle of "edge effect abundance," that "emergence happens more easily out on the fringes of organizations, where old paradigms have less control" (Part Ib); and for Ronald Heifetz's balcony-and-dance-floor image and the single-, double-, and triple-loop learning of Chris Argyris and Donald Schön, on which the reading of Hamlet's reflexivity and reflection-in-action draws (Part XXXVIII).
  52. Nick Obolensky, Complex Adaptive Leadership: Embracing Paradox and Uncertainty, 2nd ed. (Routledge, 2016), drawn on in Part If for the concept of polyarchy as a paradoxical fusion of anarchy and oligarchy and "a dynamic rather than just an attribute… practised by leaders alone"; for the paradox of "letting go by holding back" and the rendering of Daoist 無為 (wú wéi) as "action through inaction," the watchful held-back hand that lets a self-organising system "oscillate between coming together and seemingly falling apart"; and for the report, via Jim Collins's Good to Great, that "a charismatic visionary leader is absolutely not required… and, in fact, can be detrimental," the visionary state being the system's emergent orientation rather than the leader's imposed picture.
  53. Peter Allen, Steve Maguire and Bill McKelvey (eds.), The SAGE Handbook of Complexity and Management (SAGE, 2011), drawn on in Part If for Alicia Juarrero's argument that the leader of a complex system "is more like that of a catalyst than a clockmaker," working through "context-sensitive constraints" (the modern form of Aristotle's formal cause) rather than direct efficient causation; and for Paul Cilliers's "ethics of provisionality," which values "transgression, irony and an aesthetically informed imagination," and Juarrero's lesson on the importance of "looking at the overlooked" — the inconspicuous, peripheral, and hidden — and of obliquity over "direct, frontal, rational approaches" to complex systems.
  54. Gilles Deleuze and Félix Guattari, A Thousand Plateaus: Capitalism and Schizophrenia (1980; trans. Brian Massumi, 1987), for the distinction between the arborescent and the rhizome and the six principles of the rhizome (connection, heterogeneity, multiplicity, asignifying rupture, cartography, decalcomania), as summarised by Mark Bonta and John Protevi and discussed in Paul Cilliers's chapter of the SAGE Handbook; with Manuel DeLanda's A Thousand Years of Nonlinear History (1997) on change and transformation as intrinsic to form (Part XXXIX).
  55. Suzanne Simard et al., "Net transfer of carbon between ectomycorrhizal tree species in the field," Nature (1997), and Simard's Finding the Mother Tree: Discovering the Wisdom of the Forest (2021), for the "Wood Wide Web" of mycorrhizal networks — decentralised, resilient, rerouting fungal webs connecting trees across species; the bidirectional, non-random transfer of carbon, water, nutrients, and warning signals; and the "mother trees" that, when dying, increase resource transfer to seedlings through the network. The term "Wood Wide Web" is owed to Nature's 1997 headline and to Robert Macfarlane; the mycorrhiza/rhizome tabulation in Part XXXIX follows the user's own (Part XXXIX).
  56. Manfred F. R. Kets de Vries, "The Organizational Fool: Balancing a Leader's Hubris," Human Relations 43(8), 1990, and related writing on the sage-fool, the wise fool, and the office trickster as countervailing power and instrument of a leader's reality testing; Stewart Clegg, Miguel Pina e Cunha, and Arménio Rego, "Speaking truth to power: The academic as jester stimulating management learning," Management Learning, 2022; Beatrice Otto, Fools Are Everywhere: The Court Jester Around the World (University of Chicago Press, 2001); and the attribution to George Bernard Shaw of the aphorism that every despot needs one disloyal subject to keep him sane (Part Ib, Part XXXIII).
  57. Debra E. Meyerson, Tempered Radicals: How People Use Difference to Inspire Change at Work (Harvard Business School Press, 2001), on "outsiders within" who are "not fully in the box" and who "speak truths that have been suppressed"; and the wider organisational literature on positive (constructive) deviance, healthcare rebels, contrarians, and heretics as agents of emergent change (Part Ib).

All quoted matter from these sources has been kept under conventional fair-use thresholds and paraphrased throughout in the body text; direct quotation, where used, is limited to short, attributed phrases.
